The role of (big) Data Analytics in the audit process through the positionpractice perspective - An evaluation of the necessary skillset of an auditor

Valentina Tallqvist, 36891
Pro-gradu thesis in Economics
Mentor: Jean-Claude Mutiganda
Faculty of social sciences business and
economics
Åbo Akademi
Åbo 2021

1
ABSTRACT
Author: Valentina Tallqvist
Specialisation: Auditing
Supervisor: Jean-Claude Mutiganda
Title: The role of (big) Data Analytics in the audit process though the positionpractice perspective - An evaluation of the necessary skillset of an auditor
Abstract:
In the last five years, the Audit Industry has experienced major changes in the
collection and analysis of data within the audit process, leading to a change in the
audit process itself concerning data management. This evolution has been
discernible both in terms of different data programs and positionings within the
company. This study aims to evaluate what type of role Big Data Analytics and
Data Analytics have within the audit process, what type of impact it has on the
auditor and the future of auditors' skill in the audit process and why it has been
implemented in the audit process by the audit company.
This paper has evaluated the role of Big Data Analytics and Data Analytics as a
case study, conducted with qualitative semi-structured interviews. To understand
the impact both data proficient subjects, i.e. data specialists or Audit
transformation team members, and non-data proficient team members, i.e.
managers of the audit team or other audit team members, were interviewed. The
result was analysed with the help of the position-practice theory.
The case study found that Data Analytics is a part of the audit process in the form
of data cleaning and organising, and visualisations of the general ledger, such as
entries per month or cross-analysing of accounts connected to sales. The element
of positioning can be seen in the existence of the Audit transformation team,
where a specific group has been created to perform a specific task within a specific
system. As for the audit team members’ own experience, the position-practice
theory illustrates issues in the praxis, resulting in a lack of trust in both capabilities
and positionings. Out of the position-practise perspective, there is a system of
communication and power, but with the lack of proper sanctions, the individual
with the data specialist positioning feels that the capabilities are underutilised and
has a lack of trust in the system. The required skillset of an auditor requires the
auditor to have skills in Data Analytics in terms of data cleaning and organising,
data visualisation and different types of trend analyses.
The result suggests that there is a gap of knowledge between the audit team
member who orders the data analysis and the team member who performs the data
analysis. If audit companies are to implement Data Analytics, it cannot only be the
entry-level positions that are educated but those at the very top must be educated
as well.
Key words: Data Analytics, Big Data Analytics, audit process, data utilisation,
position-practice
Date: 17.5.2021

Pages: 71

1. INTRODUCTION
1.1. Introduction
Imagine you are on a boat going over the Atlantic. You have never sailed across the
ocean, or any ocean for that matter, and the boat is quite unfamiliar as well. You
know your objective is to get across the ocean, alive, but you are unsure of which
direction you go, and which necessary turns you should make. Your mission is to
steer the boat, so you must find out where to avoid going and where you absolutely
must go. Before you start your journey, you must assess the potential risks you will
face ahead and how to tackle them.
This is the equivalent of beginning an audit of a company. The engagement leader is
the captain, and it is the audit team’s responsibility to determine the risks of the
company and set the course towards what evidence should be inspected so that when
the auditor reaches the other side of the ocean of information about the company and
the industry, the completed audit is of high quality and certainty and all potentially
incorrect information has been checked.
The plotting of the course is of ever-increasing value within a society with huge
amounts of data and transactions to be inspected. Fast development within data
collection and cloud storage have led to companies having more and more capacity
to collect and store information today, which in turn means that the auditor has both
the capacity to assess risk more accurately, while at the same time lacking the correct
methods to keep up. Therefore, the auditors on the market today must have a greater
analytical mindset and a broader knowledge base, covering both analytical thinking
and data management in addition to auditing.
With a technological and digital evolution of some 30 years - from mobile
calculators along with pen and paper in the 1980s to Excel, automation,
digitalisation, and now cloud computing in 2020 - the audit industry has met great
changes. The auditor today cannot only have the ability to audit but must also have
an analytical and technical ability, which might change the requirement for the type
of auditors that graduate and are being employed.
However, while the auditors have more information to inspect, they have less time to
do so, and with that comes mistakes in the audit which increase audit risk and

2
impacts audit quality. New methods are introduced to analyse more data, partly to
solve this problem, but the time constraint impacts the learning curve of these
methods and could therefore further decrease the audit quality. The data given by the
client must be managed, processed, and utilised correctly, which becomes
increasingly important, since it gives the direction of the audit a structure and, if it is
improperly conducted, attention might be entirely misplaced.

1.2. Problem discussion
In 2015 the accounting and audit industry saw a sharp influx of academic articles on
the topic of Big Data, due partly to a conference the American Accounting
Association (AAA) held in September 2015. The 200 attendees were both academics
and representatives from the Big Four realm, and the concluding thoughts held by
the participants were that Big Data within accounting and audits is coming, and
current auditors are unprepared for the change. Later that year, PwC declared that the
company was then undertaking a pilot study to apply Big Data techniques to audit
clients, and Deloitte published on their website that they intended to invest several
hundred million dollars into Data Analytics and artificial intelligence. EY
emphasised that they believed that the structure of the audits will change drastically,
due to the possibility of entire population inspections (possible due to Big Data, and
Big Data Analytics), and that the way auditors conduct audits will change quicker
within 5-10 years. (Alles, Gray, 2016)
Five years have passed since the conference, and as per their websites, every Big
Four company currently has a department for Data Analytics. However, due to
competition, they keep what kind of technology they are utilising undisclosed, and,
therefore, it is somewhat unclear how they utilise the technology. While many
articles discussing Big Data within the audit process have been published since 2015,
only a few articles have been published regarding the impact the potential utilisation
has had in recent years.
Aside from the technology and data amount existing, the need for application in
certain areas might have become a necessity. Finland is among the countries in
Europe with the strictest requirements to be obligated to perform an audit, where
companies are obligated to perform an audit when their balance sheet exceeds 100

3
000 €, their sales exceed 200 000 € or when the total amount of employees exceeds
three employees. This is quite exceptional since most countries in Europe have
equivalent requirements of 800 000 €, 1 000 000 € and a minimum of 50 employees.
(Nikulainen et al., 2019) .
In addition to having a larger amount of companies to audit, a Finnish auditor must
conduct their audits within the timeframe that the company must turn over their
financial statements to the Trade Register, i.e. within two months from the point in
time when the financial statements were determined. Together with the financial
statements, the company must also release the audit report, which is the final product
of the audit. The audit itself must be conducted according to the Finnish audit quality
standards set in the third chapter of the Audit law (Revisionslag 1141/2015) and
according to international standards set by The International Auditing and Assurance
Standards Board (IAASB) and The International Standards of Auditing (ISA).
Therefore, the audit must be made on a large number of different companies, within
a certain timeframe and fulfilling a certain level of quality. To uphold these
standards the auditor must conduct a comprehensive audit of the financial
statements, which reflects the financial situation of the audited company.
For companies with larger financial statements, the amount of information that is to
be examined requires more sophisticated methods and analyses to possibly know
where to look. Furthermore, ISA requires in article 520 “ANALYTICAL
PROCEDURES” that the auditor must use analytical procedures (among others)
within the risk assessment and as a substantive procedure. Big Data Analytics has
therefore a clear entryway into the audit and is therefore beginning to raise the
question of whether the methods used are adequate. With these new sophisticated
analytical methods more data and information can be analysed, visualised, and
interpreted, but with new technologies comes false interpretations and misunderstood
results.

1.3. Purpose of the study
The purpose of this study is to evaluate what type of role Big Data Analytics and
Data Analytics has within the audit process, and what type of impact it has on the
auditor and the future of auditors' skill in the audit process. The motivation of this

4
thesis is to establish how analytics is utilised and how it impacts the auditor, and
how that affects what the future of auditing looks like concerning Data Analytics.
This thesis looks into the whole audit process, since analytics is a new concept
within auditing, and it would be limited to the thesis itself to only examine a single
part of the process. The time pressure in combination with the infinitely larger
amounts of information collected and under examination, suggests Data Analytics
might be the solution to uphold a high-quality audit. Because an audit is dependent
on having a clear structure and requires an acute awareness of each person's
responsibility in the audit team, this thesis applies the position-practice perspective.
Position-practice is part of the structuration theory tree, where relationships are
analysed in terms of duality – rules and resources (Gibbens, 1984).
My research questions are:
-

Is BDA or DA introduced as a method and why is it introduced in the audit
process?

-

How does BDA or DA affect the audit judgement in the audit engagement?

-

What type of skillset is required of the next generation of auditors in terms of
analytical skills?

It is imperative to investigate this due to the rapid development of storage,
processing and collecting of data, at the same time as the auditors are required to
perform high-quality audits. Furthermore, this paper is needed since it provides
researchers with the position-practice theory, an approach seldom used in accounting
research. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
1.4. Boundaries and limitations
Since BDA is relevant only when large amounts of information need to be processed,
this thesis concentrates on audits made on large companies, according to the
definition by the Accounting law chapter 1, §4 C1(sv. Bokföringslag 1997/1336).
The thesis only concentrates on audits of group companies for the same reason. This
thesis focuses on the entire process, since limiting the thesis to one area would

1

” For the purposes of this Act, large corporations are liable for accounting for which at least two of
the following three limit values are exceeded on the closing date for both the closed and the
immediately preceding accounting period: 1) total assets of EUR 20,000,000: 2) turnover of EUR
40,000,000: 3) the average number of employees during the accounting period is 250 people. (BFL
chapter 1, §4c.)

5
prevent the reader from getting an overview of the application of Data Analytics.
When observing this from the audit quality perspective, this thesis follows the
IAASB definition of audit quality. This thesis focuses only on external audits on
financial statements.

1.5. Structure of the study
Firstly, this thesis aims to give an introduction of the audit process and audit quality
for the reader to understand the process of the audit and give an overview of where
analytics could be or are used. After that, the thesis gives the reader a walkthrough of
the analytical procedures that are required of the auditor, according to ISA 520. This
is followed by an explanation of Data Analytics within auditing, followed by an
explanation of Big Data. Thereafter, previous research of (B)DA within auditing is
presented followed by the thesis method, a case study through qualitative interview.
The interviews are analysed first in relation to the research question, then previous
research and finally through the position-practice theory's perspective, referencing
the findings in the research question analysis and the analysis of the research
questions.
Thereafter, the results are presented, concluding first with a comparison of the
qualitative research to the previous study conducted, and second with the final
thoughts, containing limitation and future research as well as the final discussion.
Are auditors prepared for this type of technology? Has the new type of analyses
proved the previous analyses wrong or inadequate? When this type of statistical
analysis has been used, what type of problems have arisen?

6
2. THEORETICAL BACKGROUND FOR THE RESEARCH AREAS
2.1. The role of the audit and the structure of the audit process
To understand the implications that Big Data Analytics and Data Analytics used
within the risk evaluation have on the audit quality one must first know how the
audit process is structured, why audits are conducted, and the quality requirements of
them. This chapter goes through why and when an audit is conducted, what regulates
the process and thereafter it delves deeper into each step. This is followed by a
walkthrough of the regulation of audit quality as well as its measurement.
2.1.1.

The purpose of an audit

Audits are performed from two perspectives – externally and internally. The internal
audit is performed from inside the organisation by the organisation. It is an audit
commissioned by the management of an organisation to review the organisation's
administrative and financial information systems to properly convey the instructions
given by the management and instructions to the organisation, and to provide the
management with reliable, sufficient and relevant information about the results of
operations. The purpose of the internal audit is also to examine whether the internal
monitoring systems are appropriate and that they function as intended. It is supposed
to decrease mistakes made within the organisation, improve the organisations'
economic situation and safeguard the assets of the organisation. (Jyrkkiö, 2000)
The external audit, on the other hand, is performed by an external entity for the
organisation. According to the Finnish auditing law (Revisionslagen 1141/2015),
every Finnish company that exceed certain standards are obligated to perform an
external audit on their financial statements. External auditing comprises all statutory
audits regulated by law. The primary purpose of an external audit is to ensure the
correctness of the accounts and the adequacy of the information contained therein.
The external audit also verifies whether the internal audit has been performed
adequately and that it functioned as intended. Audits are for the markets, keeping a
sort of guarantee of whether the financial statements given by the companies can be
trusted. (Jyrkkiö, 2000)
The following chapter goes through the audit process in detail when they are made
and how the different stages are being performed.

7
2.1.2.

Process in detail

The audit process begins with planning the audit process, followed by a testing of the
systems within the entity and finished by a statement made by the auditor. The audit
consists of inspection of the accounting, the financial statements, the annual report,
and the management during the financial period under inspection.

Figure 1: Audit phases (modified from Riistama, 2000, taken from Jantunen’s thesis
2019)
Much of the manner of the audit and the manner of the auditor is regulated by law:
the auditor must produce an audit note (3 chap. 4§) and an audit report when the
audit is completed (3 chap. 5§), the audit must be completed with professional
knowledge, integrity, objectivity, and care (4 chapt. 1§), the auditor must develop his
or her knowledge (4 chap. 2§), and be responsible for maintaining the quality of the
audit (4 chap. 5§). The process itself is unregulated, according to the Finnish
legislation, but instead in ISA, International Standards of Auditing. It is here that the
audit gets its three stages: planning of an audit, audit testing, and the actual financial
statement audit. The image below gives a clear visualisation of the audit phases. The
AP stands for Analytical Procedures. (Halonen, 2010)

8
Halonen (2010) discuss how the first stage can also be called the risk assessment
since a large part of this phase involves risk assessment of the audit object and
assessment of the audit risk. The purpose of this stage is to understand the company,
its operations and operating environment as well as its internal control, and using
this information to identify and assess the risk of material mismanagement in the
financial statements. At this stage, the overall strategy of the audit is created, risk
assessment measures are taken to understand the business and the operational and
control risks identified. Furthermore, the components of the internal control are
identified, and an assessment of their planning and use is performed. All these stages
are taken from the ISA manual.
The second stage (Halonen, 2010) is the confirmation measures. The goal of this
stage is to obtain audit evidence for the auditor's assessed control risk. After the
checks have been tested and other materials are inspected, an assessment is made of
the probability of errors in the financial statements. The size and length of an audit
are determined according to the size of the audit object, and the level of the internal
control.
The final stage is the overall review. This is the stage where the auditor makes
his/her statement and notes whether the financial statements follow the regulations
and so forth. In the following chapters, this process is defined in depth. (Halonen,
2010)
2.1.2.1.

Planning and risk assessment

Audit planning is vital as the first part of the audit process since it is the activity that
sets the wheels of the audit in motion. Before beginning the testing and reporting
stage, the auditor must first have an audit plan. The purpose of the planning is to
establish the overall audit strategy for the engagement and to develop an audit plan.
(ISA 300)
The purpose of the audit plan is for the auditor to gain an overview of the entity’s
operations, the accounting system, and the internal control system and the risks and
materiality within and connected to the entity. This overview gives the auditor a
picture of nature, the timing, and the extent of the control measures to be performed
by the auditor, as well as the coordination, guidance, supervision, and job review. To

9
implement the plan an audit strategy is created, wherein the nature, timing and scope
of the audit are defined. (Horsmanheimo, Steiner, 2002)
The methods used at the audit planning stage aid the auditor in the budgeting of time
and attention, as well as direct the auditor towards potential material misstatements.
There are some activities and audit procedures that require the planning to be
completed for them to be analysed and, therefore, planning is often conducted at the
beginning of an audit. Before the identification of the risk of material misstatement,
the auditor should complete the analytical procedures of the audit. The auditor
should also have obtained a general understanding of the legal and regulatory
framework applicable to the entity and how the entity is complying with that
framework, i.e. if it applies national regulations or IFRS, which trade union the
employees belong to, among others. (ISA 300)
The risk assessment part of the audit plan falls within both the audit strategy and the
audit plan. The risk assessment is not enough to audit evidence for the audit opinion,
but is, however, essential when planning where to find the potential material
misstatements. The risk assessment shall, according to ISA 330, include inquiries
with the management and others within the entity who is likely to assist in
identifying risks of material misstatement due to fraud or error. Apart from the
inquiries, the risk assessment should also include analytical procedures as well as
observations and inspections. This thesis concentrates on the analytical procedures,
which are more thoroughly explained in chapter 2.2 Risk Assessment.
One Big Four company describes their planning as sectioned into planning, risk
assessment and audit strategy and plan. The planning begins with a formal
acceptance of the client by the audit firm, then verifying compliance with
independence requirements, building the audit team, and performing other
procedures to determine the nature, timing and extent of procedures to be performed
to conduct the audit effectively. The risk assessment is performed using the auditor’s
knowledge of the business, the industry, and the environment in which the company
operates, to identify and assess the risk of material misstatement. Once the risk has
been assessed, the auditors develop a general audit strategy as well as a detailed
audit plan to address the risk of material misstatement in the financial statements.
(PwC, Understanding a financial statement audit)

10

2.1.2.2.

Inherent risk (IR)

The inherent risk is the first step in the risk assessment process. It is here where the
auditor first determines is there a risk for material misstatement, i.e. any level of
error, within the company, regardless of internal controls. It is separated from
internal controls because it is the act of determining whether there is any risk, to
begin with within the entity. Control risk in turn checks whether the internal controls
have caught these mistakes.
The official definition of inherent risk by ISA 200(13§ n. i) is that inherent risk is the
risk that an assertion of a transaction, account balance or disclosure to a
misstatement could be inaccurate. The transaction, account balance or disclosure to a
misstatement could be material both individually or collectively with other
misstatements and could be so regardless of any related controls. When judging the
inherent risk, the auditor is determining whether there are significant risks of
material misstatements within the company.
Determining significant risk means in practice that the auditor should at least
evaluate whether there lies a risk of fraud within the company. The auditor should
also consider finding out if there is a recent specific economic or accounting
development that could lead to a risk of misstatement. For example, the Finnish tax
declaration site for payroll tax has recently changed from MyTax to Palkeet.fi, which
has forced many small companies to digitalise, many before they are ready (Sipola,
2019).

2.1.2.3.

Control risk (CR)

Improvements in economical units, technological progress, increased turnover, and
lack of resources among others have made effective internal controls an integral
component of efficient management systems. When auditing financial statements,
the effectiveness of internal controls is considered a fundamental step in the auditing
process (Shanssadeh, Zolfaghari, 2015). The internal control of entities has gone
through quite an extensive history to get to where it is today. Scandals such as Enron
and the 2008 financial crisis has led to frameworks such as COSO(The Committee of

11
Sponsoring Organisations of the Treadway Commission) and the Sarbanes-Oxley
act. COSO defines internal control as “the plan of enterprise and all of the coordinate
methods and measures adopted within a business to safeguard its assets, check the
accuracy and reliability of its accounting data, promote operational efficiency, and
encourage adherence to prescribed managerial policies.” (Moeller, 2014)
The auditor, on the other hand, must judge the overall and account level materiality
partly based on the control risk. The ISA 315 defines internal control as “the process
designed, implemented and maintained by those charged with governance,
management and other personnel to provide reasonable assurance about the
achievement of an entity’s objectives concerning the reliability of financial
reporting, effectiveness and efficiency of operations, and compliance with applicable
laws and regulations”. Control risk (CR) is therefore the risk that the processes fail,
and material misstatement occur. (ISA 315)
The auditor must gain an understanding of the internal control to assess CR by
evaluating the control environment (Shanssadeh& Zolfaghari 2015). ISA 315 raises
this as to whether the management and those in a similar position have created and
maintained a culture of honest and ethical behaviour, and whether the control
environment elements provide an appropriate foundation for other components of
internal control. The auditor must also gain an understanding of the risk assessment
within the entity (Shanssadeh& Zolfaghari 2015). ISA 315 mentions this as to
whether the entity has a process for identifying business risks relevant to financial
reporting objectives, processes for estimating the significance of risks, processes for
assessing the likelihood of occurrence, and processes for deciding about actions to
address those risks.
Finally, the auditor must gain an overview of the information and communication
exchange and the monitoring of these (Shanssadeh& Zolfaghari 2015). This is
mentioned in ISA 315 as an obligation for the auditor to obtain an understanding of
the information system, including the affected business processes, relevant to
financial reporting. Furthermore, the auditor must gain knowledge of the transactions
within the entities’ operations that impact the financial statements. These are
transactions such as cash flow related transactions and storage valuation. The auditor
must also understand the procedures within both IT (information technology) and
manual systems with which transactions are recorded, processed, corrected as

12
necessary, transferred to the general ledger, and reported in the financial statements.
The auditor must also understand how the entities information systems capture
events and conditions other than transactions that impact the financial statements.
The auditor must also gain an understanding of how the entity prepares its financial
statements as well as significant accounting estimates. Finally, the auditor must
understand how the entity has set up its control measures surrounding journal entries.
The auditor shall also obtain an understanding of how the entity communicates
financial reporting roles and responsibilities and significant matters relating to
financial reporting. In practice, this means the communication channels between
management and those charged with governance, as well as external
communications, such as those with regulatory authorities. (ISA 315)

2.1.2.4.

Audit evidence

Audit evidence is the material that the auditor uses as the basis for his/her
judgement. The collection of audit evidence is constant, through the whole process,
and cannot, therefore, be sectioned into a specific time frame. Audit evidence is
regulated by ISA 500 “Audit evidence”, where it is defined what amount of evidence
is sufficient, what information can be used as evidence, what type of testing methods
should be used to obtain evidence and discuss inconsistencies in audit evidence.
When the planning and risk assessment has given light to which areas the audit
should concentrate on, the audit evidence from these areas is collected. Audit
evidence is defined as information used by the auditor in arriving at the conclusions
on which the auditor’s opinion is based. The evidence includes both information
contained in the accounting records underlying the financial statements and other
information, such as procedures within the company. (ISA 500)
Imagine that you want to make an argument that the valuation used for the fixed
asset is flawed. You would have evidence to support this claim, which means that
audit evidence, in this case, are the calculations used by the entity for the valuation
of the asset. The audit evidence is relevant already at the planning stage since the
auditor must at this stage consider the relevance and reliability of the information
that might be used as audit evidence. The audit evidence in form of incoming data
used is financial statements and reports, the internal control systems, and bank

13
records (Riistama, 2000). It is collected by testing the internal control systems and
by inspection of financial statements, among others. PwC has stated that they collect
evidence through a combination of testing the company’s internal controls, tracing
the amounts and disclosures included in the financial statements to the company’s
supporting books and records, and obtaining external third-party documentation.
(PwC, Understanding a financial statement audit)
2.1.2.5.

Testing

The testing of the entity’s systems is the second stage of the audit process and begins
when the planning and risk assessment have shed light upon which areas of the audit
have the highest risk of material misstatement. The control and testing part of the
audit process is sectioned into inspection of the entity’s bookkeeping, the financial
statements, and the administration. These are inspected by investigations of the
systems and by investigations of the records (Horsmanheimo, Steiner, 2002). Areas
of inspection could be sales and accounts receivables, storage, cash flows and
payment transactions. The purpose of the testing is to check whether the statements
made in the financial statements are true, or if there exists material mismanagement.
(Tomperi, Käld, 2005)
The actual testing is sectioned into two categories: control procedures and planned
procedures (Riistama, 2000). Control procedures consist of control testing, while
planned procedures consist of a test of details and substantive testing. Controls are
tested if the auditor, at the risk assessment stage, considers them to be necessary to
test, or if the auditor considers substantive testing to give insufficient audit evidence
at the assertion level. Control testing is the test of systems working properly by
testing a specific attribute, such as if the date on an invoice matches the date in the
systems, or whether the approval process for salary is performed by the correct
people. (ISA 330)
Planned procedures are the procedures performed to gain sufficient appropriate audit
evidence regarding the assessed risks of material misstatement, through appropriate
responses to those risks. There is also a test of controls at this stage, as well as
substantive procedures, which divide into test of details and substantive analytical
procedures. The purpose of substantive procedures (or substantive analytical
procedures), is to establish whether there is misstatement or fraud at the assertion

14
level. For example, the auditor might examine whether the depreciation of fixed
assets calculated by the client is calculated correctly by performing the calculation
and investigating potential differences. Analytical procedures (which are discussed
later in this thesis) is one step further, considering previous financial years and
relationships between financial and non-financial information, such as “payroll costs
to the number of employees” (ISA 520).

2.1.3.

Audit Quality

To understand the implications Big Data Analytics has on audit quality one must
first know what is meant by audit quality and how it is relevant when choosing
analytical methods in audits. The auditor is the one who is guaranteeing the audit
quality, so therefore this chapter gives a deeper understanding of what the auditor’s
responsibilities and duties are.
Since audit quality is more conceptual than it is tangible, it is hard to define and,
therefore, difficult to regulate. The lack of regulations on audit quality has led to
much happening “under the table” so to speak, and currently, there are many
different audit boards and international regulations regulating audit quality to ensure
that the audit reports which are given can be trusted. This chapter goes through the
different definitions of audit quality. Firstly, the IAASB definition of audit quality is
presented, followed by different definitions given by other institutions and in
literature.
IAASB is The International Auditing and Assurance Standards Board, and the
framework of audit quality is based on ISA 220 “Quality Control for an Audit of
Financial Statements” and the International Standard on Quality Control (ISQC) 1,
“Quality Control for Firms that Perform Audits and Reviews of Financial
Statements, and Other Assurance and Related Services Engagements”. In Finland,
the legal adoption of the International Standards on Auditing (ISA) as issued by the
IAASB. However, according to the Finnish Association of auditors, Suomen
Tilintarkastajat ry, de facto ISA rules are being applied. Therefore, this section
presents the framework given by IAASB for audit quality.
IAASB argues in their published guide “Framework for audit quality” that audit
quality consists of five key elements: inputs, process, outputs, key interactions

15
between the stakeholders within the financial reporting supply chain and context
factors. These key elements are further sectioned into engagement level, i.e., the
perspective of the individual auditor, firm-level, meaning the perspective of the firm,
and national level, from the perspective of the national regulations.
Scholars have also given audit quality a similar definition. Baskin and Ramamoorti
have in their article “The Gresham's law of measurement and audit quality
indicators: Implications for policy making and standard-setting” (2017), as well as
IAASB guide, categorised audit quality into three groups. The first group is audit
professionals, with which is indicated the different measures to dealing with the
availability, competence, and focus of the auditors who are performing the audit. The
second group is the audit process, which is a measure of the audit firm's tone at the
top and leadership, incentives, independence, as well as attention to infrastructure,
and record of monitoring and remediation. The third and last group is about the audit
results, which enclose the financial statements, internal control, going concerned,
communications between auditors and audit committees, and enforcement and
litigation. This categorisation is quite like the IAASB definition, where the first
group would be the IAASB input, the second group the process, and the third group
the output. The quality process in the framework follows Riistama’s (2000) structure
of the audit, in this case, planning is the input, testing being the process, and output
is the conclusion.
Analytical procedures are used at the planning stage, and, therefore, require some
form of standard of quality in terms of inputs and process. The next chapter presents
analytical procedures, which are regulated in ISA, for the reader to understand what
type of analytics is used in audits, and how, to understand where the industry is
currently at regarding analytics in the audit.

16
2.1.4.

Analytical procedures

In 2018, Appelbaum, Kogan and Vasarhelyib conducted a comprehensive literary
study of what types of Analytica Procedures have been used in auditing and the audit
process. This chapter presents the different APs used at the different stages of the
audit, how these procedures are conducted, as well as what type of information is
required for the procedures. The AP techniques are categorised into audit
examination, unsupervised, supervised, regression and other statistical methods.
They are categorised based on the type of result they produce: audit examination
produces a right/wrong answer, unsupervised methods enlighten previous events,
supervised methods provide a probable outcome of events and regression analysis
analyses how events relate to each other.
APs within the audit examinations are basic transaction tests. The examination stage
uses documentation such as the general ledger and the inventory or ledger.
Transaction tests are the inspection of for example whether a purchase of an item is
registered at all the different accounts it is supposed to be registered at. Methods
used at the audit examination stage are methods such as transaction tests, ratio
analysis and CAATS (Computer-assisted auditing techniques). While ratio analysis
and transaction tests represent one type of specific procedure, CAATS contain a
myriad of different tests. CAATS are software programs that assist the auditors in
performing data extraction, querying, manipulation, and analytical tasks needed to
perform the transaction tests and ratio analysis. CAATS have been used within the
inventory of financial statements, to calculate the realised and statistical net loan
losses and so forth. To perform these tasks the program needs both structured and
unstructured data, in the form of inventory (structured) and agreed sales contracts
(unstructured). Big Four auditors are far more likely to use CAATS than smaller
firms because they require more resources, which the Big Four tend to possess.
(Gambetta, García-Benau & Zorio-Grima, 2016)
The category Unsupervised consists of techniques that conclude data sets in which
the output is unknown, in contrast to transaction tests where the result is either that
the transaction is correct or not. Unsupervised methods describe something which
has already happened. This means that there is no clear result of the test, for
example, whether a transaction is trustworthy or fraudulent, but rather gives an

17
implication of which result differs from the norm. Methods used within this
categorisation are clustering, text mining and visualisations (Appelbaum, Kogan &
Vasarhelyi, 2018). Text mining is used to extract relevant information from a large
amount of text data, by turning big text data into a small amount of relevant text data
(Zorio-Grima, Carmona, 2019). Clustering (categorising a set of objects in the same
group that are more similar to each other than to other groups) and visualisations are
more descriptive since these methods utilise visualisations to highlight issues.
Clustering can be used in combination with text mining, by clustering the most
frequently used words to know what topics are most discussed, and pattern
recognition. (Madani, 2015)
Supervised is the opposite of unsupervised, where the output is known, and the test
gives a direct answer. Supervised methods describe something which could happen
using already existing data. Supervised methods draw conclusions based on labelled
data sets also called training data, meaning the test expects a certain type of result. In
auditing labelled data sets could be the general ledger or the inventory, where data is
presented in a structured way with labels of what the different data means. Typical
supervised tests used at the risk assessment stage are process optimisation, expert
systems and decision aids, Bayesian theory/Bayesian belief system and probability
models (Appelbaum, Kogan & Vasarhelyi, 2018). Expert system and decision aids
are mechanical optimisation methods for otherwise menial tasks that aid the auditor
in conducting decisions, intended to improve the effectiveness of decision-makers by
supporting the process without automating the whole process (Abdolmohammadi,
Mohammad J., 1987). The purpose of the probability model, on the other hand, also
called the probabilistic model, describes audit engagement as an analytical process,
with definitions of the important characteristics which propose an overall measure of
audit quality. Early probability models used “statistical decision and sampling
theories focusing on evidence gathering for detecting misstatements”, while later
models viewed the audit task in the context of agency theory. The latest probability
models now go by game theory (Laitinen, 2015). Bayesian theorem or the Bayesian
belief system is a type of probability model which measures the probability of
something happening by relating it directly to the problem (the probability of it not
happening). (Bolstad, 2016)

18
Regression techniques show possible relationships between events: no direct answer
but gives an overview of the situation. Regression methods used at the risk
assessment stage are log regression, linear regression, time series, ARIMA,
univariate and multivariate (Appelbaum, Kogan & Vasarhelyi, 2018), while Glover,
Drake & Prawitt (2015) comments “regression … provides a direct method to
measure the level of confidence while maintaining a threshold equal to materiality”.
Other statistical methods used besides audit examination, unsupervised, supervised
and regression is methods that do not fit the criteria of the previously mentioned
categories. Methods used in this category are multi-criteria decision aid, descriptive
statistics, and structural model. (Appelbaum, Kogan & Vasarhelyi, 2018)
All these tests are stated to be used within the auditing industry in some capacity,
however, all these tests require an extensive amount of data, in different forms such
as structured (hard numbers) and unstructured (text, speech, illustrations) at a high
speed (within the first three months after the closing of the books). Analytical
procedures represent the analytical part of Data Analytics within auditing, while
Data Analytics consists of analysing numbers, data collection and data cleaning
(more on this in the following chapter). As the capacity for the collection, storage
and processing of this type of Big Data has increased drastically in the last ten years,
a new type of data processing methods must be introduced to be able to handle all
this information in the form of Big Data Analytics. BDA programmes are the type of
technology the current age of auditing is facing, and, therefore, this next chapter goes
through what Big Data is first, then what is meant by Data Analytics, as well as
giving an illustration of how exactly data for audits are a perfect representation of
BD. Lastly, it presents how Big Data Analytics is used for the analytical procedures
ISA 320 requires of the auditor.

19
2.2. (Big) Data Analytics
To understand the role Big Data Analytics has within the risk evaluation process, one
must first know what is meant by Big Data and Data Analytics. This chapter explains
the background and history of BD, the technical definition, what role it has in today's
society and finally what Big Data Analytics is, the different programs and the
positive and negative sides of these.
2.2.1.1.

Audit Data Analytics

Data Analytics is the collection, cleansing and analysis of raw, structured and
unstructured data with the aid of software. Within auditing, there is a concept called
Audit Data Analytics (ADA), which is the usage of Data Analytics within auditing
(Guide to Audit Data Analytics, 2017b). Since this thesis aims to understand the
usage of Data Analytics within audits, it reviews the concept of Audit Data Analytics
in this chapter for the reader to understand what is meant by Data Analytics within
auditing.
There are three steps in ADA – the collection of data, the preparation of data into a
format the auditor can use and the analysis of the data. Programs such as Alteryx or
SQL are used to convert collected data in a table format provided by a client into a
format that is uniform and, therefore, comparable. One problem one might encounter
is where in one system the dates might be separated by commas (), while in another
it might be separated by points (.), and with 759 584 562 excel rows in each file, it
becomes difficult to unify these “by hand”. (Agresti, 2013)
These systems also place different types of data into different variable types: binary,
nominal, ordinal, interval variable. Binary variables have only two possible
outcomes: the variables are either true or false, the result is either “Yes” or “No”,
“Success” or “Failure”. Variables with more than two outcomes, which have no
particular order to follow, are nominal, such as types of products, for example,
“accessory”, “vehicle”, and “clothing”. Ordinal variables, in turn, can be placed on a
scale, such as dates. Interval variables include the type of information which has a
relation, whose order is relevant, such as profit, or cost of the product. When the data
is uniform and correctly categorised, the data is ready for analysis. (Agresti, 2013)
This analysis of the data is the transformation of data into information, based upon
which decisions can be made and conclusions are drawn. Within the analysis of the

20
data, the auditor can determine key attributes, identify data with special
characteristics, perform value estimation, identify anomalies and correlations, or
relationships. This is done by using graphics and tables, in other words, with
visualisations. Certain types of graphics are often linked to certain types of analysis:
when performing a regression analysis, the auditor might use a scatter plot and a
trend line to better visualise the results. (Agresti, 2013)
One might use bar charts to discover anomalies between employees. If the auditor
then would want to know whether Chong just happens to have more entries, an
analysis would be conducted of the comparison with the number of journal entries
per creators able to create journal entries (AICPA, 2018). In this case, Chong did not
have many entries or dollars journalised, so the auditor might perform a test of
details particularly of entries created by Chong (AICPA, 2018). More advanced
visualisations could be made by using external data, by comparing the market index
for a specific sector to a company within the sector.
Data Analytics in auditing is possible due to two factors: technological development
and audit automation. Information can now be stored digitally and accessed through
cloud-based systems since the cost of storage of information has radically decreased
during the last few decades. What previously was stored on paper is now digitised in
excel, and the files which previously were transported on memory sticks or disks are
now sent to the auditor over the internet. Audit automation began in the 1980s but
has only recently begun to utilise the vast opportunities automation can provide over
a wide variety of audit procedures. (Kogan, Mayhew & Vasarhelyi, 2019)
One way of using Data Analytics effectively in the audit process is, instead of audit
testing with sampling, that the whole population would be tested, i.e. population
analysis. Currently, data is being selected for testing with sampling techniques such
as non-statistical and statistical, statistical being the choice of the largest events in a
data source, and non-statistical being the random selection of samples to attain a
picture of the whole data source (Abdolmohammadi, Mohammad, Wright, 1987).
Only a fraction of the population is tested to attain an overview of the population
and, therefore, much data remains “under the radar”. One way to test the whole
population with Data Analytics would be with the MADS (Multidimensional Audit
Data Selection) framework. The purpose of the MADS model is to guide the auditors
towards the identification of outliers for the substantive tests of details. The model

21
contains data collection, preparation, and analysis, which is then used in
implementation (audit testing) and evaluation, the basis for data analysis. (No et al.,
2019)
Audit Data Analytics helps auditors to obtain an insight into the company under
audit with visualisations and graphics, widens the amount of data to be tested in
terms of full population analysis, and has many other applications. However, Audit
Data Analytics only focuses on structured financial data, purchase records and data
in cohesive applicable files. However, when the data analysis must be taken in a
different direction, to begin to use unstructured data in the form of text and pictures,
the capacity of Data Analytics ends, and the possibilities of Big Data Analytics
begin.
2.2.1.2.

Big Data

The history and development of Big Data is the history and development of data
management. The better data can be managed the more data can be collected.
(Ohlhorst, 2012) characterises Big Data as “a situation in which data sets have
grown to such enormous sizes that conventional information technologies can no
longer effectively handle either the size of the data set or the scale and growth of the
data set”, clearly indicating that without proper data management, Big Data cannot
exist and be utilised. The data need to be managed, and the data managers need to
know which database mechanisms to use since with BD traditional database
mechanisms has become inadequate with storing, processing and analysis of the data
(Siddiqa et al., 2016). Therefore, to understand what Big Data is, this chapter goes
through the three waves which laid the groundwork for what Big Data is today, with
the help of data management.
BD's properties were in 2012 defined as the three Vs: Volume, Variety and Velocity,
but only seven years later BD's properties expanded with three additional Vs:
Veracity, Variability and Value (Al-Turjman, 2019). This part of the thesis
introduces and explain BD by going through the six V:s, followed by a practical
example of Big Data.
BD is data defined by six properties: data volume, data variation, data rate (velocity),
data accuracy (ambiguity), data variability and data value. Data volume refers to the
amount of data generated, which includes that available data must be assessed for

22
relevance. Usually, it is the question of terabytes (1024 GB) and petabytes (1015
terabytes) (Kaiser, Armour & Espinosa, January 1, 2013)
Data variation, on the other hand, is the different formats that the data can come. The
data can be either structured in an easily processable format such as excel or R, or
unstructured, such as board minutes, sales contracts or press releases. (Al-Turjman,
2019)
Data velocity (velocity) refers to the speed at which the data is generated. Data can
be generated in real-time or manually processed. The data can come from online and
offline sources. The data collection can thereafter be classified into batch and stream
processing. It should be noted here that speed also has to do with data reliability.
Imagine analysing the stock market price: even if you enter the information manually
and update manually, the information you entered can no longer match when you
start analysing. Therefore, the data collection must be automated. (Al-Turjman,
2019)
By data accuracy (veracity) is meant the reliability of the data. The data collected
and analysed may be spotted by bias or abnormality, of poor data quality. It is
assumed that the data must be analysed, and irrelevant and unreliable information
filtered out. However, this can be difficult if the data is collected in streams of
information and can go through and find relevant information. (Al-Turjman, 2019)
Data interchangeability is the lack of the ability to find consistent data or data that
follows a pattern. Unlike data variation, this is judged if the data concerning all data
analysed is regular and reliable. The last V, data value, refers to the value of
preserving and storing data if the data is worth investing in infrastructure (clouds) to
be preserved in. (Al-Turjman F. 2020)
Big Data is also being processed in a certain order. The information (data) needs to
be collected, the data needs to be processed, the processed data needs to be managed,
and finally, the managed data needs to be analysed and generate solutions. (AlTurjman F. 2020)
What consists of Big Data Analytics
When Big Data started to gain popularity, the subfield of Big Data Analytics also
rose. Data Analytics, or BDA, is the translation of a vast amount of data into
understandable and potentially useful information. This chapter goes through what

23
BDA is, then presenting the requirements of implementing it in businesses, as well
as the strengths and weaknesses it possesses.
(Elena Geanina et al., 2012) explained the many dimensions that differ BDA from
traditional data-processing architectures. One of these is the speed at which decisions
can be made, the complexity of the analysis and the problem, the amount of data and
the variation of data. BDA can process larger amounts of both structured and
unstructured data, in contrast to traditional data-processing techniques that are
hindered by the type, and amount, of data. Big Data Analytics are therefore specific
applications and properties which are made to analyse, prioritise, and manage BD for
business purposes (Dong, 2020).
Rajaraman V. divides BDA in his article Big Data Analytics (2016) into four
categories: descriptive, predictive, discovery and analysis to improve. Descriptive
analysis refers to the transformation of data of what has happened and happens to bar
charts, graphs, pie charts and more to easily understand large amounts of data
visualised. Such can be seen in the division of one country's population by age,
gender, education, income and so on. With predictive analysis, upcoming events are
predicted based on conclusions drawn from already existing data, such as statistics,
neural networks from machine learning. This type of analysis is widely used for
market analysis to find out the customer's needs and preferences.
Exploratory analysis is the search for unexpected correlation between parameters in
a collection of BD. With this kind of analysis of data from different sources, you can
discover new insights and patterns. One of the greatest trait discovery analytics can
provide is precisely the analysis of data that shows patterns, which gives knowledge
and insights which can be realised. These are methods such as text mining, where
often used words in a large amount of text is extracted and used for conclusions.
When predictive analysis responds to what can happen in the short term, discovery
analysis can respond to what can happen in the long term. The last category, analysis
to improve, identifies based on data possible to optimize solutions to identified
problems. This type of analysis is most often used to maximize profit. (Rajaraman,
2016)
BDA has as well been defined as a phenomenon that requires a culture where
technology, analysis and a data-driven atmosphere can work together (Dremel et al.,

24
2018). (Gupta, George, 2016) divided these into three dimensions of the capability to
utilise Big Data Analytics: tangible, human and intangible. The tangible capability
requirement is that there is data (both internal and external), there is technology
(Hadoop, NoSQL) and there are basic resources, such as time and investment. The
human capability requirement assumes managerial skills in the form of analytical
acumen, and technical skills, in the form of education and training to Big Dataspecific skills. The required intangible capabilities are a data-driven culture, with
decisions based on data rather than intuition, and an intensity of organisational
learning, with the ability to explore, store, share and apply knowledge.
Within auditing, BDA can be applied by including non-financial data into the audit
and use more advanced data techniques such as visualisation and predictive analysis
into the process as methods used. One of the Big4, PwC, had in 2016 used Big Data
techniques as a pilot study. The reason why BD has major potential is that with BD
and BDA the auditor can assure and get a far more extensive idea of who the client is
and what the client does, compared to the mere financial statements currently used.
(Alles, Gray, 2016)

Figure 2 (Alles, Gray, 2016)

BD in auditing is, according to Connor 2012, the combination of transactions,
interactions, and observations. As an example, BD would be the combined analysis
of purchase records, offer details, and user-generated content. For example, if an
auditor were to do a risk assessment on the audit object, (s)he could with text mining
analyse the customer reviews, and if there are many mentions of a bad product or
returns, greater emphasis would be put on the purchase records to check if they are
up to date and see if the product supplier has sold other products that could be faulty.

25
2.3.Theoretical framework – position-practice
This chapter presents the position-practice perspective, which is a subgroup of
structuration theory, a process where agents selectively use structures to generate
specific actions (Mutiganda, 2016). The central concept of the theory is that
structures are dual, as the structures are both the medium and the outcome. In this
theory, the position-practice perspective argues that within the structure, there are
"institutionalised positions, positional identities, the sense of prerogatives and
obligation" (Elbasa, Wright 2017; Stones, 2005: 63). A structure could be any type
of setting where there is some sort of organisation performing tasks, such as an audit
company, a specific committee, or a board of directors. This chapter presents the
structuration theory and its components, followed by an examination and
presentation of the position-practice theory with its respective components. This is
followed by a presentation of how the theory has been applied in the accounting
industry.
Anthony Gidden defines the central concept of structuration theory as the duality of
structure (Coad, Glyptis 2014), where the structure is the concept within which
action is produced, and each action is a production of something new. The structure
is connected to the past, on which it builds, and expands (Adams, Sydie 2001).
Within this theory, Giddens emphasised three elements: (human) agency, which is
the deciding factor, reflexivity, "the monitored character of the ongoing flow of
social life", and structure, the continually reconstructed result of rules, resources, and
agency (Adams, Sydie 2001). This theory was developed to interpret research
problems and their results, and within accounting, the research used to understand
accounting policies and practices with social and political phenomena. The
structuration theory was later split into four categories, to be able to tackle
structuration from different angles: external structures, internal structures, active
agency, and outcomes (McPhail, Moore 2016: Stones, 2005). External structures
refer to the social positions, their associated practices, and networks of social
relations (McPhail, Moore 2016: Greenhalgh and Stones, 2010). The internal
structure is further sectioned into conjecturally specific and general-dispositional.
The conjecturally specific relates to the position in position-specific structures,
related to the external structure. The general-dispositional is, in turn, transitional,
connected to habits and routines, and not limited to a position. The active agent is a

26
cooperation between both external and internal structures. (McPhail, Moore 2016:
Coad, Herbert 2009)
Giddens points out further that while "(s)ocial systems only exist in and through the
continuity of social practices ... their structural properties are best characterised as
'position–practice' relations" (Coad, Glyptis 2014). The position-practice theory
consists of four elements: praxis, positioning, capability, and trust.
Praxis is defined by Coad and Glyptis (2014) as the process produced through social
actions, including the nature, conditions, and consequences of said actions, in other
words, a "process by which capabilities are enacted or practised by individuals to
achieve their interests". Praxis is the process within which specific actions are
executed and in this thesis, praxis is represented as the audit process, a process with
clear positions, where the capabilities (resources and rules) are implemented and
used. Praxis can be exercised in three different forms: power, communication, and
sanction. Power may be political, involving organisational units or social systems, or
economic, consisting of available resources or technologies. Communication is the
creation of shared meaning in interaction, while sanction is the consequence of
actions, the "approval and reward or disapproval and punishment" of actions. (Coad,
Glyptis 2014)
Positioning relates to the specific interactions of an "identity" with a network of
social relations and the obligations and prerogatives related to the "identity" (Coad,
Glyptis 2014). This means that within a structure identities exist which carry many
hats: boyfriend, manager, father, friend, board member, football player, and data
expert. These roles have different expectations and expected behaviour from
different parties. The identities are all connected to the prerogatives and obligation of
the identity, meaning power is drawn from both the position the identity holds and
the structure the identity is within. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
Giddens has identified three types of capabilities which represent the rules and the
resources the individuals within the positioning and praxis use when "engaging in
purposeful behaviour". In this definition, Giddens assumes that the individuals
engaging in purposeful behaviour are knowledgeable and reflexive (Coad, Glyptis
2014). The elements of capability are the different types of skills the identity uses to
understand its environment based on the resources and the knowledge of the

27
structure, to either succeed or fail. The first element is the "capability of actors to
make use of established practices embodied in organisational and interorganisational routines", meaning the capacity to understand the organisation and
make use of the different possible practices within the organisation. The second
element of capabilities represents the capability of actors to derive service from
resources, and the last is the capability of actors to envisage future relations within
the structure, with the same type of positionings. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
According to Giddens, social systems cannot exist without trust: it is a fundamental
part of proper coordination and must be included in structuration-based studies of
accounting and control (Coad, Glyptis 2014). Coad and Glyptis define trust as an
"assumption of the continuity of that with which we are familiar and is evident in
institutionalised practices". Trust is always connected to some form of risk on the
trusting party. Trust is often local, meaning one does not usually trust another agent
or a system in every respect, but specific, concerning a certain type of behaviour.
Trust allows for agents to have specific expectations of other agents future
behaviour. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
The case study began by analyzing the reason behind the political decision-makers
using Data Analytics when performing audits, and the praxis within which it is
implemented. The political position-taking was contrasted with position-taking
among both data proficient audit team members and non-data proficient audit team
members. Finally, it compares the managerial relationships between political
decision-makers and the audit team members when using Data Analytics, and their
expected future change in behaviour, regarding the capability of the audit team
members.

28
3. PREVIOUS RESEARCH OF (B)DA WITHIN AUDITING
This chapter presents the previous research conducted of (B)DA within auditing. The
chapter is sectioned into two parts: the role of (B)DA in the audit process, and the
auditors’ competence and required skillset for the audit in regard to (B)DA.
3.1. The role of (B)DA in the audit process
According to research conducted by Dagilienė and Klovienė (2019), Big Data
Analytics tools have been part of the audit process for approximately three years. It
is, however, unclear what these methods are in practice and, therefore, most likely
interchangeable with Data Analytics (DA). Therefore, it is notable that when BDA
(Big Data Analytics) is reviewed here, it may also indicate DA (Data Analytics), as
the distinction between the two within the audit world is yet unclear.
BDA is often introduced as part of the company strategy of IT innovations, with the
main purpose as “ensuring the quality of the audit process and to issue a relevant
auditor’s report” (Dagilienė & Klovienė 2019). Significant resources have been
invested in research designed to explore potential applications of Big Data
visualisations in the audit process, and BDA in forms of data visualisation belongs to
one of the fastest-growing practice areas at the larger offices of the Big Four firms
(Rose et al., 2017). In a more recent article, Dagilienė & Klovienė (2019) comment
further on how large audit companies such as Big Four currently consider BD as an
ever-increasing “essential part of their assurance practice”. This chapter presents
previous research on Big Data Analytics within auditing.
Before going into the specifics of how the accounting methods push auditors to adapt
with analytical tools, the thesis gives a walkthrough of what has been written about
the current auditing practices in combination with the potential of BDA. This has
been done for the reader to understand why the “usual” methods might in certain
contexts no longer be adequate.
Krahel and Titera explained in their article “Consequences of Big Data and
Formalisation on Accounting and Auditing Standards” (2015) that while data is more
available and data collection is more standardised, the auditing standards continue to
focus on methods such as sampling and similar practices. These practices are no

29
longer, according to Krahel and Titera, relevant in a data-intensive environment and,
therefore, standards must address “atomistic errors and their appropriate response”.
Rose et al. (2015) comment how Big Data has the potential to completely change the
way auditors collect evidence and make audit decisions (Rose et al., 2017 BrownLiburd et al. 2015). In this type of Big Data environment, the analytical methods
used to interpret the data (BDA) would not only be applicable, but preferable
(Krahel, 2015). Griffin and Wright supported, in their article “Commentaries on Big
Data's Importance for Accounting and Auditing” (2015), the statement that the
methods used in auditing for straightforward tasks such as sampling, will transition
into population analysis and make audit risk rely on real-time Data Analytics. An
issue they however explained is that there is a lack of adequately trained specialists
in auditing Big Data-based ERP systems.
Further, according to Brown-Libburd, Issa and Lombardi (2015), the time auditors
currently allocate to familiarise with a company before the audit is wasteful of time
and largely unnecessary when an analyst easily could conduct a quick analysis
instead. Were data-collection procedures to transition from manual processes, such
as going through company records and financial statements, to automatic processes,
such as natural language processing, the auditor would have more time to
concentrate on complex tasks. A practical example of this is when Humphreys et al.
in 2011 used Bayesian text analysis to improve fraud prediction based on word
choices in the management discussion and analysis section of the financial reports
(Brown-Liburd, Issa & Lombardi, 2015). Miklos, Vasarhelyi, and Kogan (2015) also
stresses that as audit objects’ systems increase in both size and complexity,
assurance of these will require more automated audits. At the same time as the
auditing profession is lagging in the use of Big Data techniques, the profession has a
large and growing, the volume of data of significant variety and veracity to review
(Gepp et al., 2018). As auditors in general work with structured data, the pressure to
be able to audit more significant data sizes increases as companies themself are
drastically changing their internal audit process by using BDA (Rose et al., 2017). In
fact, in a study made in 2019 by Klovienė and Dagiliene, it was discovered that the
main drive to use BDA techniques was because large business companies created
and allowed for suitable conditions to use Big Data, in the form of financial and nonfinancial, structured and non-structured data, for audit purposes. The large

30
businesses tended to be innovators in the application of BD and the audit industry
tended to be the followers.
Klovienė and Dagiliene, (2019) also explained that research indicated that “business
companies had used BDA tools for more than five years” and considered this as an
“increasing trend in the future because of strong competition, and these tools were
used to understand the customers’ behaviour, to manage risk and for internal
management purposes”. The same article explained that audit companies, in turn,
had merely used BDA for three years.
Some researchers tackled where BDA could be applied and where the largest
fundamental change would be, I.e. the ability to test the entire population instead of
getting an overview of the population via sampling. By using the entire population in
an unorganised dataset, the auditor would focus more on correlation than plausible
relationships. (Cao, Chychyla & Stewart, 2015)
Another area of application where BDA greatly could improve the performance is
within analytical procedures. BDA could yield more insightful and reliable results,
which in turn improves the decision-making process. For example, auditors could
conduct regression analysis on the entire population, instead of merely the sampling
size, and, therefore, improve the model’s usage and explanatory power, as well as
the identification of anomalies. (Tang, Karim (2019))
Current auditors are currently dealing with having to collect, analyse, and synthesize
large amounts of data sets from many different sources, to form an audit opinion on
potential errors or risk of errors, with methods that might be out of date (BrownLibburd, Issa, Lombardi 2015). Krahel and Titera (2015) explained further that error
detection and risk quantification are no longer sufficient targets, but that the current
massive volume of data and, with the detection of risk, potentially a large amount of
exception reports, requires a change within the reporting and assurance methods.
According to Krahel and Titera (2015), while companies are increasingly automating
their processes, deeper data analysis in combination with data availability must be
introduced into the auditing process to ensure a more thorough and higher quality
audit. Auditors have the ability, and soon the duty, to improve the audit judgement
significantly with the help of Big Data, by being able to eliminate sampling and

31
replace it by utilising technology that can both retrieve and examine the whole
population under inspection (Brown-Libburd, Issa, Lombardi 2015).
Big Data Analytics has both a potential within the audit process, as well as in more
specific areas such as sustainability reporting and fraud detection (Tang, Karim
(2019), Wanner, Janiech (2019)). Within fraud detection, management manipulation
could show on many different data sources, and, therefore, the integration of
unstructured data into the inspection improves the “veracity of audit evidence”. This
means that data collection in both large quantities and from different sources produce
a more extensive evidence base than traditional data collection and sampling (Tang,
Karim (2019)). Another area of application for BDA is within sustainability
reporting. In a study conducted by Wanner and Janiesch on “Big Data Analytics in
sustainability reports: An analysis based on the perceived credibility of corporate
published information”, the authors concluded that the credibility of the
sustainability report could improve the completeness and accuracy of the report with
the help of BDA.
3.2. The auditors' competence and required skillset for the audit concerning
(B)DA
This last section of previous research goes through previous research of the impact
Big Data and Big Data Analytics have on the auditors’ skillset. Rose et al (2015)
explained that no matter how many promises BD must offer auditors, there is limited
understanding of what type of impact BD has on the auditors’ judgement and the
decision-making processes.
The areas where BDA become costly are many. It is expensive to implement and
invest in BDA tools, as well as in from of operating costs, such as keeping the
systems up to date, the analysis of the data, the collection of the various types of data
and the storage of the collected data (Tang, Karim (2019), Shukla, Mattar (2019)).
Costs also stem from when the performed tests give false positives, and auditors
must go back and redo tests (Krahel, Titera (2015)). It is costly to have the tools
themself, as well as competent personnel also require compensation, due to the lack
of competent auditors with experience within Data Analytics. It also seems to be a
trend for companies to underinvest in Information and Communication Technology
(Shukla, Mattar (2019)). BDA could on the other hand diminish cost, since it could

32
enhance the audit process, by reducing the amount of time spent on a specific audit
(Salijeni 2019 p. 20). I failed to find previous research conducted on the cost of BDA
concerning the benefits BDA potentially brings for the auditing process.
It has also been stated many times that there is a lack of competency and educated
auditors that are familiar with, and able to use, BDA. Alles and Gray (2016)
explained that there must be a change in the analytical/technical skills of the audit
team, and in a study conducted by Klovienė and Dagiliene, on motivation to use
BDA, an interviewed auditor disclosed that apart from the auditor having an
accounting and audit education, they must also have an IT competence. Makla and
Shukla (2015) also emphasised that if BDA is to be implemented into the process,
analytical skills are crucial for the implementation to be successful. (Brown-Liburd,
Issa & Lombardi, 2015) also emphasised that one of the major challenges in
implementing BDA is just that there is a “lack of the adequate training and required
skills to analyse Big Data”.
There is pressure from the accounting world for audit firms to incorporate BDA into
the audit process, as well as from individual audit quality regulators to use more
advanced analytical tools in the audit process, to make the risk-based audits of better
quality (Dagiliene and Kloviene (2019)). However, when introducing new tools into
an already time-constrained process, problems in correct utilisation might arise. In
this case, the same type of advantage that Big Data provides the auditor pose an
equally great challenge: being able to use Big Data and Big Data Analytics for audit
judgement and decision-making (Brown-Liburd, Issa, Lombardi 2015). It has been
known for some time how vital the auditors’ ability to recognize patterns is, to assess
audit risk. Previous research shows that auditors fail in recognizing patterns in
financial and non-financial data, a clear indication that there is a lack of competence
in the Big Data audit era. Auditors fail to recognize patterns as well as adequately
use historical information to identify patterns and use their findings for their audit
judgement. (Rose et al., 2017)
Rose et al. (2017) explained that there are factors that could improve the auditor and
the audit environments ability to recognize and utilise BDA fully. These are factors
such as having a framework that instructs and aids the auditor to identify and
interpret patterns, using visualisation strategies, the order in which the information is
processed as well as be aware of personal biases and the limitation of human

33
cognition (Rose et al, (2017), Griffin, (2015)). Brown-Liburd, Issa, and Lombardi
(2015) stressed that expert systems and predictive analysis could be tools to further
aid the auditor in patten recognition, as well as information overload, helping in
sorting our irrelevant information and ambiguity.
As the reader may notice, there is no clear distinctive place where BDA could be
applied and used or the actual effect it has on the auditor. A dear child has many
names, and it is evident that BDA is at the stage of confusion regarding the
definition and application in the auditing industry, and how it differentiates from
DA. This thesis aims to get an understanding of the actual use of BDA and DA
within the audit process, I.e. how these analytical tools improve or could improve the
auditors’ judgement and problems that arise with the interpretation of data. I research
both DA and BDA since first and foremost the line is blurred between the two
procedures, and secondly, since they are so closely aligned, a clear picture of how
Data Analytics is used could open up a framework for BDA application.
Something that must be mentioned at this point is that most of the previous research
done on BDA within auditing has been conceptual: meaning that there is a common
opinion among scholars that BDA, with its limitations and hurdles, are to have a
great potential benefit on the audit and the auditors’ judgement, but have yet to
examine the current practical use of BDA. Therefore, this thesis aims to get an
insight into the actual potential use of BDA within auditing, to find out whether the
scholars are right in its usefulness and limitations, how it is applied, and if the use of
BDA requires the next generation of auditors to have an analytical education as well.

34
4. RESEARCH METHOD AND EMPIRICAL STUDY
As I have previously mentioned in the problem discussion, every Big Four company
have a department for Data Analytics, and every one of them stresses how Data
Analytics is to the future of the audit. They preferred to keep their usage of Big Data
Analytics as a competitive secret, so I asked each through simple email whether they
did. However, due to competition, they respectfully declined to reveal the inner
workings, or what technology they are working on, and, therefore, there is somewhat
of a blind spot in terms of how they utilise the technology. There is therefore a need
for practical research of the usage of Big Data Analytics within auditing, and,
therefore, I have conducted a qualitative, revelatory case study using cognitive
mapping of the usage of BDA and DA within the audit process of an anonymous Big
Four company through semi-structured interviews.
This chapter goes first through the qualitative research method and the reason behind
this chosen method, the difference between qualitative and quantitative research, the
type of case study to be performed, how the data is collected, the interview questions
