and lastly critique of the chosen method.
4.1. Research questions
The type of qualitative research used in conducted research is the cognitive mapping:
research with the assumption that “people interpret data differently and they will
therefore understand problems in different ways” (Bryman, Bell 2015). The primary
function of cognitive mapping is as a tool for reflective thinking. Below you can see
the structure of the cognitive mapping process taken from Eden, Ackermann, and
Cropper (1992) by Bryman and Bell 2015.

Figure 3 Byman and Bell 2015

35
I have conducted qualitative research according to the six main steps of qualitative
research. I have gathered research questions, which are
-

When is BDA introduced as a method and why is it introduced?

-

How does BDA affect the audit judgement of the risk assessment in the audit
engagement?

-

What type of skillset is required of the next generation of auditors in terms of
analytical skills?

I have selected a relevant site in form of a Big Four company and subject in form of
a new type of method used in the audit process. I have collected relevant data by
interviewing professionals within the relevant field of Data Analytics within the risk
assessment. I have interpreted the data in the analysis chapter and finally concluded
in the result chapter. My thesis, therefore, follows the framework of qualitative
research.
4.2. Research method
Qualitative research is often segregated into six main steps. Qualitative research is
inductive research: first, gather material, then establish a theory, which is then
proven through empirical research. The opposite, deductive research, form a
hypothesis, followed by analysis to prove, or test, the hypothesis. The main steps are
sectioned into gathering research questions, selecting a relevant site and subject,
collecting relevant data, interpreting the data, conceptualising the work done and,
lastly, writing up findings, and compiling a conclusion. (Bryman, Bell 2015)
Qualitative and quantitative research have many similarities and the distinction is
unclear. Both research methods answer research questions and conduct analysis of
the research literature, both treat frequency as a starting point for analysis, and both
investigate variances. Regardless of whether one uses a qualitative or quantitative
method, the researcher must perform data reduction. (Bryman, Bell 2015)
However, there are many differences between qualitative and quantitative research.
While quantitative research concentrates on numbers and raw data, qualitative
research analyses the spoken and written word. Another distinctive trait of
qualitative research is the closeness to the research subject – it is the close contact
that enables the researcher to gain a full understanding of the research subject.
Quantitative research has the opposite approach, by distancing the researcher from

36
the subject and by attempting to understand the subject with minimal or no contact
with the subject. They also differ in the type of result the study produces: qualitative
research is often specific to certain circumstances, and cannot be generalised, while
quantitative research tends to be able to make generalised statements due to the
amount and variance of data examined. However, while quantitative research is
precise, qualitative research gives results with great context due to prolonged
involvement. (Bryman, Bell 2015)
Due to the fact I am researching how one type of company uses (B)DA within the
audit process and how they perceive the usage, qualitative research is more apt than
quantitative research. The nature of the study requires in-depth research into the
different implications and consequences the usage of (B)DA has, confirming that
qualitative research is more fitting for this type of subject, compared to qualitative
research.
4.3. Interview process
Data can be collected for a study in several ways, and the type of research design
does not correlate with the research method (qualitative or quantitative). There are
five types of research designs: experimental, cross-sectional, longitudinal, case study
and comparative design. The type of research varies depending on the method
chosen. For example, a typical qualitative cross-sectional research is “interviews or
focus groups at a single point in time”, while a typical quantitative cross-sectional
design is a structured observation on a sample at a single point in time. The typical
qualitative case study is studied by qualitative interviewing of an organisation, and,
therefore, I have conducted my case study through qualitative interviewing.
(Bryman, Bell 2015)
Data was being collected for this thesis by conducting a case study with structured
interviews. A case study is a detailed and intensive analysis of a single case, on a
single organisation, location, person, or event. A case study can be revealing a
critical case, a unique case, a revelatory case, a representative case, or a longitudinal
case. A critical case is a type of case where the researcher has a specified hypothesis,
and where the case test circumstances where the hypothesis either rejected or
accepted. The unique case is often used in clinical studies, as it examines the extreme
case in a certain circumstance. The revelatory case is “when an investigator has an

37
opportunity to observe and analyse a phenomenon previously inaccessible to
scientific investigation” (Bryman and Bell 2015 citing Yin 1984: 44). The purpose of
the revelatory study is to research something new, that has yet to be researched. The
representative case on the other hand concentrates on researching a case that
“exemplifies an everyday situation”. The last one, the longitudinal case, concentrates
on the change of a phenomenon over time. The case study this thesis has done is a
revelatory and representative case study conducted on a single organisation, which is
possible because any type of study can involve a combination of the different type of
cases. (Bryman, Bell 2015)
As the purpose of qualitative interviewing is to understand the subject's
interpretations, qualitative interviewing is the appropriate method for this study,
since it aims to ascertain an overview of the current situation from the auditor's
perspectives. An interview can be either unstructured, semi-structured and
structured. While unstructured is more dialogue than an interview, the semistructured has previously determined questions, which aim to give the conversation a
sense of direction. The third version is the structured interview, which has a predetermined set of questions that every subject answer (Byman, Bell 2013). The
structured interview is the type of interview that as opposed to standardised
questions, the interview is a type of guided conversation, to let the auditor calmly get
to the crux of the matter. (Gubrium, Holstein 2001)
The interview is sectioned into three parts by dividing it according to the research
questions. The first research question (Is BDA or DA introduced as a method and
why is it introduced in the audit process?) aims to establish what the company policy
regarding analytics within the company is, how BDA is or could be implemented in
the process, how the audit company uses DA within the company (criteria's, type of
tests), for what purpose it is used and its necessity regarding audit quality.
The second research question (How does BDA or DA affect the audit judgement in
the audit engagement?) tackles questions regarding who prepares and cleans the data
used, and who should prepare the data, does the auditor feel the audit according to
current standards is adequate, or is there too much information to be reviewed, the
auditor's personal need for more accurate methods in the form of BDA, the auditor's
own experience and competence in regard to DA and BDA, if the auditor considerers
DA needs to be used more, and lastly the positive and negative effects of DA.

38
The last research question (What type of skillset is required of the next generation of
auditors in terms of analytical skills?) aims to discover the future auditors'
capabilities: if it is the employers or the educator's responsibility to educate the
auditor of new technology, whether it is enough for auditors to have accounting and
audit skills, or do they also must have IT or statistical competence. It also aims to
understand whether the current auditors equipped for this type of Data Analytics.
4.3.1.

The conducted interview approach

As previously mentioned, the research was conducted through interviews over
Google Meets. This type of computer-assisted interviewing was necessary due to the
Covid 19 pandemic. The interviewees were invited to the interviews by calendar
invite, after an email confirmation confirming that the subject is willing to be
interviewed. This meant that the interview had a clear beginning and an end.
Gubrium and Holstein 2001 explained that this type of clear beginning and end with
CAI (Computer-assisted interviewing) makes the subject happy that their work is
done when the interview is completed. In total the interviews summed up to ten h
and 22 minutes, twelve subjects, and the average interview length about 52 minutes.
I transcribed with the help of Sonix, a program that turns audio into text. It
transcribed with 50-70% accuracy, and, therefore, I transcribed the last remaining
50-30%. The interviews were analysed with the help of Excel, where the answers
were clumped into a table format and compared on a question basis.
Each interview began with an introduction of how the interview was going to be
conducted, as well as introductory questions for the interviewee about the interview
subjects working period at the company, education, position and whether the
interviewee was an authorised auditor or not. The interview questions can be found
in Appendix I and the transcribed interviews in Appendix II. Since all the
interviewees had Finnish as their mother tongue, the interview was conducted in
Finnish. Because a semi-structured interview is “an interview with the purpose of
obtaining descriptions of the life world of the interviewee in order to interpret the
meaning of the described phenomena” (Kvale & Brinkmann, 2008: Brinkmann
2013), this interview was semi-structured, because the purpose of the interview was
to interpret the role, and meaning of the role, of Data Analytics.

39
As previously mentioned, this thesis has conducted the case study of a single
organisation by interviewing employees within the Big Four audit company that
works with Data Analytics within the audit process. The case study has been
conducted by interviewing auditors that are “data-specialists” and auditors that are
“ordinary”, meaning they work and have experience only within accounting and
auditing. The interviews were conducted one on one by video meeting. The
individuals chosen for the interviews were chosen in cooperation with the Audit
Transformation team (a subdivision within the company) as appropriate candidates.
Since the interviews were conducted as semi-structured interviews, each subject has
been asked the same set of questions, however, since the employee background is
diverse, some interviewees decided to leave some questions unanswered. The openended questions were followed by follow up questions when needed. The interview
subjects have been chosen purposely from different backgrounds, as “normal”
auditors, meaning auditors that work on audit engagements who either order or are
otherwise in touch with the Data Analytics results, data specialists, which were
auditors with the title data specialist, responsible for the execution of data analysts
and lastly directors or managers responsible for introducing Data Analytics into the
audit process who further continuously are trying to discover new methods to
introduce into the audit process. The variation of subjects was chosen to get an
overview of the different perspectives who are in touch with Data Analytics, and,
therefore, get at more diverse and extensive research.
Further, since this is my first time conducting a qualitative case study with semistructured interviews, the order of the interview questions differs slightly from
interview to interview, as well as which all questions were asked, and which follow
up questions were asked.
The interview questions were both fill in the blank and open-ended, as well as
binary. The first introductory questions were all fill-in-the-blank questions and
binary, while the questions related to the research questions were either binary or
open-ended.

4.4. Reliability, replication and validity

40
I chose the qualitative method because I am trying to give an insight into the
possibilities and limitations of the direction the audit industry is heading towards.
For that, I need to discuss with professionals within this field and get their
perspective on how this type of technology aids, limit and improves their work. A
simple survey would have been insufficient for this thesis with the depth of
perspective and context, and, therefore, it is conducted as interviews of subjects. I
chose the case study because it allows the thesis to do a deep dive into how a single
organisation tackles these types of issues and possibilities, especially since the
development of this type of technology requires the environment to be positive
towards change.
For the conducted research to be of high quality, it must have a high level of
reliability, replication and validity. Reliability means that the research must be
trustworthy, that results would be possible to replicate, was the research to be
repeated. This requirement is more relevant in quantitative research than qualitative
research, where the study is often a discovery of whether a measure is stable or not.
In this qualitative study, reliability is achieved by the conclusion that this is a case
study, where replication is possible if the same limitations are used when the study
would be replicated (company, time and subjects). Replication is achieved by the
capability of replication – it must be able to be replicated in another setting. In this
case, it is very possible to do the same research in another Big4, or do comparative
research between them, to get an overview of where the audit industry stands today.
Validity is the criteria that there is integrity in the conclusions that are drawn from
the thesis. The conclusion's that are drawn are reliable thanks to the variety of
interview subjects in combination with unified answers, as well as the similar
environments between the Big Four audit companies (Byman, Bell 2013). The next
chapter goes through the result of the interview, followed by the analysis of the
result and the final discussion.

41
5. RESULT
This chapter goes through the result of the interviews. The first chapter presents the
company is chosen and the interview samples. The following chapters present the
interview structure.
5.1. The research entity and the interviewees
The sample for this thesis consists of twelve interviewees from company X.
Company X is a Big Four audit company, located in Helsinki. Company X has in the
last five years made major changes in its audit process regarding data collection,
processing and analysing. The company has globally invested 3 million euros during
the next four years into Data Analytics, and one major change is that starting the year
2020, all employees in the regional Finnish office has five days a year to educate
themself in Information Technology, or what they call Digital Upskilling. Further,
they have during the year 2020 introduced a new tool, program Y, created to test
huge volumes of data, analyses whole populations to improve risk assessment and
perform analysis and testing.
“... there has been quite a huge change in the last five years from when I started my audit career. It
was unfortunate that the standard job was the report that what was obtained from the customer was
pdf because it was thought to be so much easier to read and interpret and deal with … and maybe the
auditor preferred this to excel, and did not notice it to be an issue ... a really big change on the part of
both their customers and the auditors in a very short time which has worked in our favour. This
change made it possible for Data Analytics to be used” - B (Manager Data Specialist)2

I have defined the interviewed auditors according to their position in the company.
They are sectioned into Data proficient positions and non-data proficient positions.

Seven(half) of the interviewees works with Data Analytics, as the role Data
Specialist or leader of data specialists. Data specialists work with Data Analytics as a
role complementing their position. Some are working as well on projects to
introduce new Data Analytics technology in the audit process (A and C). One is the
Audit Transformation leader, audit transformation being the department responsible
for continuously trying to find new ways to improve the Audit process.
Most of the Data Specialists started working at the same time as the field of Data
Analytics emerged as a tool in the audit process, about 3-4 years ago. Among the
non-data proficient interviewees, all but one were an HT or CGR auditor. The
auditor without CGR or HT, interviewee K, was chosen to get a perspective of the
current auditors who work as team members and are neither the one who lead the
team (as G-J do) or perform the Data Analytics (as D-F do), but are a representative
of the “normal auditor”.
The following chapters go through the result of the study per the research questions.
The chapter begins with a section explaining what was aimed to be discovered with
the research question, followed by the result the interview answers gave in relation to
the question, and each chapter will end with a conclusion of the answers regarding
the respective research question.

44
5.2. The role of DA or BDA in the audit process
The first research question (Is BDA or DA introduced as a method and why is it
introduced in the audit process?) aims to establish what the company policy
regarding analytics within the company is, how BDA is or could be implemented in
the process, how the audit company uses DA within the company (criteria, type of
tests), for what purpose it is used and its necessity regarding audit quality.
What is the company policy regarding analytics?
The two directors G and H considered the main corporate strategy for Data Analytics
to be something that is a key part of the audit and that it will be emphasised more,
and that the company invested time and money into extensively increasing
knowledge of Data Analytics. Practically, this means integrating Data Analytics with
the already existing business services. There is no company policy on the actual
implementation of the Data Analytics and, therefore, it is still at a case-by-case
consideration, dependent on the team leader of the audit whether it is to be used. It is
clear, however, that there is no company-wide knowledge of the strategy regarding
Data Analytics. Interviewee A observed that there is no single strategy for
specifically Data Analytics due to the fact that it is merely a tool: " se on vain yksi
työkalu siellä monien joukossa". Data Analytics is included in the overall strategy
for the audit transformation, and how the company sees auditing in the future.
One practical implementation of the overall strategy for audit transformation is the
Digital Upskilling program for the employees, where each employee has five days
per year to concentrate on the improvement of the employee digital skill. The
purpose of the Digital Upskilling, among others, is for the company's future auditors
to understand where the use of data is sensible and efficient. Interviewee B
emphasised that many changes have been made in the past years, where before there
was much talk but no action, and now there is a company-wide educational program
to educate the employees. The regional department has invested in programs such as
Alteryx, Tableau, QlikSence and an internal program for workflow procedures, and
there is also a global initiative, GL platform, where offices around the world can
share successful workflows. Interviewees D, E, F and K thought the strategy to be
having better and more effective solution’s, to continuously develop Data Analytics
as well as be a pioneer in Data Analytics within the audit process.

45
Position-practice
Coad and Glyptis’s (2014) categorisation of capabilities within the position-practice
can be seen here: they develop and encourage the use of resources both within the
already existing systems (the audit process) and have an environment where they
create new tools for the audit team members to use both now and in the future, in
order for the team member to permanently be able to utilise all the available
resources and knowledge about them.

How BDA is or could be implemented in the process
Five of the interviewees (non-data proficient interviewees G, I and J, and data
proficient interviewees C and D) thought the audit company to be using BDA in the
audit process in some capacity. Those who answered yes considered BDA to be
limited to only structured financial data used with the tool Alteryx and considered it
Big Data due to the amount of data processed, and that a specific program (Alteryx)
was used to tackle the amount of data.
“A lot of software is used when there are such big masses (of data) with the customer, even the
general ledger, which cannot be handled in Excel, with tens of millions to hundreds of millions of
rows of data” interviewee C

The other motivations for an affirmative answer were the usage of visualisations, as
well as the upcoming Program Y. Since the auditor soon will, with some customers,
have access to the customer’s entire ERP systems, tests and analyses can soon be
performed in Program Y.
“There is a program coming up with which we would be able to directly catch the customer's entire
SAP...we would be able to fully process it (the company data) if we could get the tests from there
directly.” interviewee E

Interviewee G noted that there had been discussions on the tax consulting side of the
company regarding text mining, where contract data was scanned and key
information selected by a program, which interviewee B called Optical Character
Reading, but this technology has yet to be introduced in the audit process. The
director explained that this technology would certainly be useful in the audit process.
Most of the interviewees considered the text analysis to be the type of analysis the
audit process could have most use of. Interviewee G stressed that it would be useful

46
if text analysis was used in the form of contracts are first scanned into a program, the
contract is then converted into machine form and, with the help of it, specific themes
or words are examined. Interviewee E also stressed this, complementing it with that
the text analysis could be used to analyse board meeting minutes and interviewee G
complemented this with sale, purchase and leasing contracts, in order to discover
trends and anomalies, and with the help of them highlight certain risk factors or
trends.
Factors working against the usage of BDA are that it is still in its infancy stage and,
therefore, unusable (said by interviewee H), that the data must become more
standardised, that the source of data is still only internal data from the company and
no external data (said by interviewee A). Interviewee K said that they may use Data
Analytics with Alteryx, but not Big Data Analytics just yet. Interviewee D said Big
Data Analytics is not being used, but that most likely soon it will due to the XBRL
requirement invoked this year, by comparing the audited company with the
performance of other companies in the same field with the help of XBRL. Currently,
BDA is used in the form of processing power, by combining general ledger data,
sales invoice data and payment data in the program Y (the company's analysis tool),
a tool that, although it is in use, is very much so in its infancy as of yet, being
implemented in 2020.
Position-practice
Here the trust element of the position-practice theory can be seen, in the
interviewees trust in the system and that further development is coming, they trust
both the system (the company and its audit transformation team) to enable this type
of technology, and the positions to develop the resources. The director explained that
it would certainly be useful, indicating that when the time comes, it would be no
issue on the audit side in introducing the technology (Coad, Glyptis 2014).

How the audit company uses DA within the company, (criteria's, type of tests)
As Data Analytics is sectioned into data cleaning, data organising and data
analysing, I asked all the interviewees what type of analysis is conducted in the audit
process, at what stage in the audit process it was used and the criteria to use it. All
the interviewees agreed that for the most part, Data Analytics is used at the planning

47
phase and the conclusion phase. According to interviewee E, the practical process
for choosing DA into the audit process is the following: the audit team consults with
a Data Specialist at Q2 or Q3, present the amount and type of data the client can
provide to the Specialist, who then construct the bases before year-end and present
them to the audit team. The team then evaluates whether the tests are feasible, or
necessary and whether they fit in with the budgeted time for the client. At year-end,
when all information from the whole financial year can be inspected, the model is
updated.
Position-practice
Here is clear evidence of structuration within the audit process, with a clear
structure of audit process, which every party was aware of, and within which
processes were produced (McPhail, Moore 2016: Coad, Glyptis 2014). Further,
within the position-practice theory, positioning of the audit team can be seen, where
data-specialist are assigned a specific role they are expected to execute, that the other
audit team members can trust the individual to execute. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
Factors that affect the choice to use Data Analytics are the size of the company, and
in this case, all the interviewees referred to listed companies. Another contributing
factor is the amount of the data: if the company only have a few data entries the
point to use Data Analytics is moot, however, when the data exceeds the point when
the auditor cannot easily go through the data in excel, it is sensible to introduce Data
Analytics into the process. Enough data for analysis can also be achieved if a group
has many subsidiaries and their collective mass compound into a large amount of
data. Another factor affecting the choice to use analytics is the data availability, or
the data variability, from the client’s internal systems. If the client’s system is
outdated or fragmented and it is difficult to extract data then analytics become more
of a hinder than an aid. Further, if the data is in paper format the data analytics
would be inadequate since for the analytics the input data has to be in a spreadsheet
format. However, data in a paper format highly unusual in the present climate.
Another factor affecting the use of DA is the reproducibility of the analysis: the
tests performed are strived to be used in more than just one company. If the system is
highly specific for just one audited company, it is ineffective to plan and perform
tests that cannot be replicated on a different company. If the company's ERP system

48
on the other hand have a system such as Netvisor or Procountor, the same types of
tests can be performed on every client that has that ERP system and is of roughly the
same size and industry. Other affecting factors are how modern, harmonised and
cohesive the company's ERP environment is, as well as the cost-benefit analysis:
how difficult it would be to implement and perform Data Analytics concerning how
much time it might save. If a “normal” inspection is just as fast or effective as the
Data Analytics, there would be no point in using DA.
Lastly, the audit team itself impact the decision on implementing DA: if the team
itself lacks members that are familiar with Data Analytics or a Data Specialist, there
would be no one competent to perform the test. It is also dependant on the teal
leader: it is the team leader that decides if DA is to be used, and if s/he does not see
the benefit, they might not implement it.
Interviewee G uses Data Analytics for “different general ledger exports or
comparing inventory values with sales invoices, I.e this kind of testing activity like
this would normally be done in an inspection but it is just an enlarged sample size in
a way by taking all the data and building some data model to do that testing”. Most
common is the visualisation of the general ledger in Qliksence, to get an overview,
used by all the interviewees, as well as analysis of accounting entries. Trend analysis
is conducted in some capacity:
“Trend analysis id Certainly used...it's kind of like where the general ledger is analysed for turnover
or purchase trends monthly to discover how they have changed“. Interviewee H.
“We inspect trends in the accounting entry figures, whether they are appropriate for transactional
contexts, to know whether some samples should be chosen differently for certain tests”. Interviewee J.

Interviewee E commented however that most analyses are still only different kinds
of filtering and cleaning of the data with Alteryx, into a format so the auditor can
easily use it in the audit.
For what purpose it is used?
In the audit process,_ Data Analytics is used to increase the data comprehension and
to obtain an overview of the data to better the audit quality and cost-effectiveness
(Interviewee G). Another motivation was product development for the preparation of
future technologies: already some data amounts are too big to be handled with
conventional methods, and data analytics with its programs must be used to be able

49
to tackle the amount of data. Interviewees H, B, J, C, F and K concluded that
uniformity, coverage and cost-effectiveness were the main reasons for the usage of
DA in the audit process. Further, interviewee A (the Audit Transformation Chief
Strategist) considered the motivation to be the limitation of human capacity
regarding data management and analysis and client satisfaction. Another was the
automation of the audit process, to relieve the auditor from constant data picking,
collecting, and cleaning (interviewee B).
Necessity regarding audit quality
Of the interviewees, G, H, B, I, J, C, E and K considered that Data Analytics
increased the quality of the audit, but they did not consider it as a necessity to uphold
a high-quality audit. The auditor would still produce an audit of ISA standards, if
DA was left unused, however, it would not be as efficient or as thorough. Most
agreed that even though it is not a requirement, regulated by neither the audit
company nor by ISA standards, it is highly recommendable (interviewee C).
Interviewee E thought that the more audit work is done with data analysis, and the
more the audit process is automated, the smaller the risk of error will be.
“A quality audit can be done completely without any kind of analytics tool, but it also depends on the
size of the client. The bigger the company, the more relevant the analytics becomes.” Interviewee H
“When thinking about today's requirements and the size of the listed companies then yes we would
say that for them it is starting to be yes. It is starting to become a requirement at this point.”
Interviewee B, answering the question Do you consider it necessary to utilise DA to ensure that the
audit is of high quality?

Position-practice
Interviewees A, D, and F concluded that the same audit report can be produced no
matter if DA is used or not. Here, the praxis component of sanctions does not apply.
The use of Data Analytics is expected, but there seems to be no political power that
sanctions the audit team members if Data Analytics is not used. A sanction of the
economic power can, in turn, be seen in the distrust by clients when the audit
company does not utilise Data Analytics. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
Findings
Regarding the research question “Is BDA or DA introduced as a method, and why is
it introduced in the audit process?”, the answer based on the interviews is yes, Data

50
Analytics is a part of the audit process in the form of data cleaning and organising, as
well as visualisations of the general ledger, such as entries per month or crossanalysing of the accounts connected to sales. BDA is used in the audit at some
capacity; however, the definition, or difference, between BDA and DA was unclear
for the auditors, indicating that even though some answered that BDA is used, it
might very well be misunderstood for Data Analytics. DA is introduced as an effort
to increase the understanding of the audited company within a shorter timeframe
with more information, to increase efficiency in terms of time, and to lessen the
workload of the auditor.
Data Analytics was not considered necessary to ensure that the audit is of sufficient
quality but instead increases the audit quality in terms of the scope of the audit as
well as the accuracy of the audit statements. Though the choice to use DA is up to
the team leader, factors that impact the choice to use DA seemed to be the size of the
company, the amount of data, data availability, data variability, the reproducibility of
the analysis, how modern, harmonised and cohesive the audited company's ERP
environment is, and lastly, the audit team’s capacity itself. The interviewees’
perspective on why DA was introduced in the audit was inconsistent. Some
interviewees thought DA was introduced because it was considered a necessity for
the audit process and, therefore, would improve the audit quality. However, others
thought it was introduced as an additional service for the client.
Position-practice
In the auditing industry, Giddens’s (1984) position-practice concept of power at the
macro level concerning the usage of Data Analytics in the audit is an intricate
system. Both political and economic power is seen, political power in form of the
Finnish government and economic power in the existence of the Data Analytics
technology. The relationship is not dual: without economic power, the political
power remains unchanged, but without the political power, the economic power is
pointless. In other words, if there were no political power to audit, meaning the
government would decide that an audit is no longer required, the auditor is of no
importance for the client. However, if the economic power did not exist, the audits
would remain unchanged, not affected by the lack of economic power, since an audit
can be done without Data Analytics. The element of positioning can be seen in the

51
existence of the Audit transformation team, where a specific group has been created
to perform a specific task within a specific system. The capability element exists in
the tools and the specific structures which have been established to enable the Data
Analytics, and trust in the system itself can be felt. The interviewees had faith in the
system, and there were no doubts that would have caused them to distrust the
possible development of Data Analytics. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)

52
5.3. The auditor concerning DA and BDA
The second research question (How does BDA or DA affect the audit judgement in
the audit engagement?) tackles questions regarding who prepares and cleans the data
used, and who should prepare the data, does the auditor feel the audit according to
current standards is adequate, or are there too much information to be reviewed, the
auditors' personal need for more accurate methods in the form of BDA, the auditor's
own experience and competence in regard to DA and BDA, if the auditor considerers
DA needs to be used more, and lastly the positive and negative effects of DA.
Who prepares, cleans and analyses the data used, and who should prepare the
data?
According to interviewee G, they have Data Specialists to handle the larger masses
of data and develop more complex data models. The data specialists used to be, or
are, the audit department, and have therefore both knowledge of audits and data
management. There is a data specialist in each management team, and in every audit
team, there should be a data specialist. It is the data specialist who prepares, cleans
and analyses the data on the audit team (interviewee H). However, even if the data
specialist is the one to do the DA, it is still the auditors' liability to understand and
utilise the results (interviewee A), and due to the company's four eyes policy (four
eyes must inspect the executed work before it is approved), the other team members
bust at least comprehend what the data specialist has done. It is the normal auditor's
responsibility to be able to estimate what could be done and whether it is necessary,
according to interviewee B.
"For example, I have a team, where the ones who put the tools in order are the younger inspectors.
For example, I don't personally set up any clients into Qliksence, but it's the responsibility of the
younger ones, one of whom happened to be a (data) specialist in training.” Interviewee J

Currently, the analytics is not limited to the data analyst, but if a team member is
interested and has access to the systems, they may also perform the Data Analytics
(interviewees B, C). Interviewees, I, H, E and G considered that thanks to the
previously mentioned Data Upskilling program, in about five years every audit team
member should be capable to execute the DA, and the data specialist on the other
hand then does the “expert” level tests. Interviewees B, C, K and F however thinks it
to be a separate branch of auditing, that some auditors can specialise in, and not to be

53
expected from every auditor, the auditor must understand enough about DA to order
the tests and understand the results. Interviewee H further explained that the data
specialist must have some sort of background in auditing, and not be purely a data
scientist, because between professions, so much information gets lost in translation.
Position-practice
The positioning element of the position-practice theory is seen in the role of the
Data Specialist: there is a clear separation of positions, into those who perform the
Data Analytics, the Data specialists, and those who do not, and are not expected to,
perform Data Analytics. However, some expected this position to cease to exist
within five years, indicating that the position is not of permanent form, while some
expected the title of Data Specialist to evolve into a more permanent position. Here a
lack of praxis regarding communication seems to exist because although a program
for the data upskilling exists, the interviewees did not seem to know what the
ultimate purpose of the capability training was, making them only a part of a
system, which they did not question. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
Does the auditor feel the audit according to current standards is adequate, or
are there too much information to be reviewed, the auditors' personal need for
more accurate methods in the form of BDA?
All the interviewees considered that they got a comprehensive picture of the audited
company with the methods used currently, indicating that there is no need for change
regarding the overall audit methods. However, when they got the question about
whether there is too much information to be reviewed to get a comprehensive
picture, the answer was not as unified. Interviewee G explained that it is not the
amount of information that is the problem, but the variety of data, meaning that the
different formats and sort of data makes the audit difficult.
Interviewees C and F thought that when it comes to the listed companies, the amount
of information is beginning to increase quite rapidly thanks to automation and
robotics, which also contributes to there being a large amount of useless information,
which complicates the audit and makes it harder to get a comprehensive picture of
the audited company. Interviewee E did not consider there to be too much
information, but instead that the same information gets inspected from far too many

54
angles, with bank confirmations as an example, which often has up to five different
evidence-gathering activities.
Contrary to the opinion that the auditor has no issue getting a comprehensive picture
of the audited company and that there is not too much information to give an
extensive auditor’s report, almost all the interviewed auditors (except K, H and B)
thought that with the help of BDA the auditor would get a better comprehension of
the audited company, and considered this something they would want to have. In this
case, they referenced text analysis, being able to do a more extensive audit in a
shorter time, under the assumption that the material the audited companies gives will
be more uniform in the future.
Interviewee C explained that BDA would aid the auditor’s assessment by visualising
the entire general ledger, compared to currently just comparing accounts to each
other. Interviewee D and J thought that with the help of XBRL the auditor would see
the audited companies position in relation to its competitors, and then investigate
differences and ask the proper questions of the audited company.
The auditor's own experience and competence in regard to DA and BDA
Of the interviewees, seven worked directly or in some other capacity with Data
Analytics. Interviewee G was responsible for a couple of IT audits, as well as on the
team for creating better solutions for the audit process. Interviewee B worked with
data movement, cleaning, organising and data combination, as well as a data
visualisation in some capacity, however, the visualisation is often neglected by the
rest of the team, something interviewee B considered to be a great loss for the team.
Interviewee I conducted analysis's of the general ledger and trial balance, while
interviewee C did everything that somehow was connected to the audit, and is as
well a part of a project to bring AI into the audit, as a representant of the audit side
with the coders.
Interviewee E and F’s responsibility was to build workflows and import the data into
Cliksence, as well as data cleaning and combination in Alteryx. The rest of the
interviewees were the end-users of the result the Data Analytics produced. Of both
the auditors that work with data in some capacity and those who did not, not one had
a formal education in data science or data management. Those who worked with
Data Analytics had gotten their education through experience and self-learning, as a

55
consequence of their interest. All of them considered themself managing without
issue in their position, and some thought this to be something one could only “learn
by doing”. This, however, illustrated an issue: interviewee E and D both thought that
there was a lack of understanding between the managers, directors’ and partners and
the data specialist regarding the Data Analytics performed, making the Data
Analytics itself unutilised.
“Today I talked to a colleague about being a data specialist...I can do just about any job for as many
hours as I want … because half don't understand what I'm doing, how long it takes and how they
should interpret the performed job … I was on an assignment team at the turn of the year and I was
allocated two days to do the Alteryx base, but when I turned in my report, they didn't do anything
with that report. “ Interviewee E

Interviewee D further explained how the orders of the Data Analytics trust the results
quite blindly, resulting in a lack of scepticism to the results of the test and, therefore,
a gap in the control of the results. Even though the interviewed managers and
directors’ considered themself educated and experienced enough, the senior
associates that perform the work did not consider the orders of the tests to understand
what they asked for. This subject is further inspected under the third research
question.
Position-practice
Here, the capabilities are not utilised by all the concerning positioning individuals:
the data specialist produced with the specific capabilities related to the individuals
positioning and performed what was expected of the position, but the position which
would use the produced outcome of the data specialist did not utilise the outcome in
the praxis, system, and therefor did not utilise all the capabilities at hand. The data
specialist did not seem to have trust in the system to feel like a valued part of the
system. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
If the auditor considerer DA should be used more, i.e. if it has untapped
potential within the audit process
All the interviewees wanted to increase the usage of Data Analytics in the audit
process. All the interviewees wanted to increase the usage of Data Analytics in the
audit process. However, inconsistencies in the knowledge of the current use of DA,
within the audit company itself, were obvious. When interviewee of the current use
of DA, within the audit company itself, was obvious. When interviewee I (manager)

56
suggested improvements that were already implemented, such as access to the audit
client's entire ERP system, another interviewee (E) talked about the same suggestion
of improvement as something which was already in use. Another example was the
implementation of a robot at the invoice inspection phase, where a robot will make a
random sample from all the sales invoices and compare the information in the trial
balance to the invoice, suggested by manager B, and said to be already implemented
by senior associate F.
Interviewee D suggested a potential use of Data Analytics as whole population
testing, instead of sample testing, and interviewee E believed that everything that can
be automized, should be automized, such as taking the robotics further.
“You can define to the robot that you need to check whether this specific project number is correct,
and then, of course, you have the project numbers listed, and you can see that those project numbers
belong here to the correct project, so they've been ok to activate, and belong to this project. Such
things could be easily determined by some robot and we that way make use of the mass data.”
Interviewee F

Interviewee G thought that within ten years this type of Data Analytics will be used
exponentially more, and if you could receive an analysis with the press of a button,
instead of asking the Data Specialist to do the test, the threshold to use the analytics
will become far lower (the latter said by interviewee I). However, for the next step in
analytics, a far greater knowledge base is required, to be able to improve, develop
and implement (big) Data Analytics. The technology exists today and could be used,
but the auditors cannot just yet begin to use it, due to the lack of understanding
(interviewee C).
Position-practice
Here the auditors, both data-proficient and non-data proficient, are aware that there is
room for further development, which is in line with the third element of capability,
by envisaging future relations within the structure, with the same type of
positionings that, in this case, are the data specialists. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)

Findings
How does BDA or DA affect the audit judgement in the audit engagement? Since the
Data Analytics seemed to be limited to data cleaning and data organisation, the

57
judgement regarding all auditors is not greatly affected. The task is delegated to
specific persons Data Specialists, allocated to be the ones responsible for the
execution of the Data Analytics, and then the other auditors, the end-users, are
responsible for understanding the result. The end-users consider themselves to be
competent enough to understand and order the tasks, while some of the data
specialists consider the end-user not to be experienced/educated enough to
adequately comprehend and utilise the results. The position itself was under
discussion as well, with some interviewees believing this is how the segregation of
duties should be done, while other thought that every auditor should be capable to do
the work that the data specialist does today in the future.
The interviewees believed that the current methods properly reflect the standard the
methodology requires and that the amount of information is not a hinder to the audit,
more so the variety of information, meaning that there is no need for analytics such
as BDA. However, still, almost all believed that if BDA were to be implemented that
it would improve the overall picture of the audit and that the auditor would welcome
the development, indicating that they might not get an as clear and comprehensive
picture as they say.
The gap between data-proficient and non-data proficient became clear in the
potential use and improvement of DA in the audit process, in the non-data proficient
auditors' suggestions of potential improvements, which the data specialist agreed to
already be in use. The non-data proficient auditors seem confused on what is already
done and can be done regarding analytics in the audit process, while the data
proficient auditors complain that their work is not always utilised. Data Analytics is
not, as of yet, affecting the judgment in a great capacity, since it is still a fairly
rudimentary level in terms of execution. However, when BDA and other types of
more advanced analytics are implemented, the gap in knowledge between end-users
and those who execute will become a risk that needs to be addressed.
Position-practice
For this research question, the position-practice theory illustrates issues in the
praxis, resulting in a lack of trust in both capabilities and positionings. There is a
system of communication and power, but with the lack of proper sanctions, the
individual with the data specialist positioning feels that the capabilities are not

58
utilised and have a lack of trust in the system. This experience, however, is not
consistent within the entire system but is dependent on the type of capabilities the
positioning of the non-data proficient, i.e. how aware the non-data proficient are of
the available capabilities. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)

59
5.4. The required skillset of the auditor
The last research question (What type of skillset is required of the next generation of
auditors in terms of analytical skills?) aims to discover the future auditors'
capabilities: the current skillset of auditors in terms of analytical skills, the future
skillset of auditors in terms of analytical skills, were the responsibility of education
lies and the future role of DA and BDA.
The current skillset of auditors in terms of analytical skills
None of the auditors thought that at least most of the current auditors, working on
audit engagements, from top to bottom are experienced and educated enough to
order the Data Analytics and comprehend the results they produced. One reason for
this would be the novelty of this type of technology in combination with the
longevity of the auditors ordering the analytics. Another, emphasised by interviewee
I, is that the regulations are quite stale, and, therefore, might slow the process down.
“There's so much work left with that(analytics). That some understand and some do not. It varies but I
think it is a part of the basic toolkit among auditors.” Interviewee H

Interviewee A thought there to be a prejudice among auditors against new
technology, which hinders the development and application of new methods.
“That's a problem maybe today. The auditor doesn’t understand what is meant by analytics or what is
needed for the analytics but requires a data specialist on their team for an audit engagement. Like,
here’s the inventory list, analyse it. Not knowing what they want from that analysis.” Interviewee D

As for their academic preparedness, the educational institution, such as university or
school for applied science, does not currently equip the future auditors for analytics,
according to many interviewees(H). Many audit positions require the new trainees to
be experienced with tools such as Alteryx and Tableau, which is something that the
schools don't prepare for (Interviewee J). Currently, the data specialist position is
purely based on whose interested and available, and not who is previously educated
in some capacity or have experience (interviewee B).
“I feel that even today, the training of specialists is quite insufficient to even understand the tests we
are doing. Like I get a result, but I don't understand what is being sought for there.” (E)

The lack of knowledge means that no one questions the results. Interviewee D
stressed that he could give almost any type of results to the end-users, and they
wouldn’t question them.

60
Position-practice
The lack of capability indicated a lack of trust in the positions, and with no power
in from of sanctions, neither positive nor negative, to enforce the capabilities, the
praxis is weak. Data Analytics is underutilised because the individuals that hold the
positions as data specialists are educated enough to fully utilise the companies
resources, but since they work in combinations with those who does not have the
capabilities, the resource is underutilised. There is a need for the understanding to
expand beyond the positioning of data specialist, which is in motion due to the data
upskilling program, but this study was conducted in its early stages and, therefore,
the effect of the resource education is not yet implemented fully. (Coad, Glyptis
2014)
The future skillset of auditors in terms of analytical skills
Interviewee A thought that the absolute basics of Data Analytics should be a part of
the auditor's education. An auditor needs to understand the different information
systems(H), can see the whole picture with the analytics, or with analytical
capabilities(A). Interviewee B thought that it is no longer enough to only have
auditing and accounting skills in today's audit world when the audit client themself
work with business analytics and robotics.
Manager J explained that to be able to get a more extensive picture of the client and
make the audit more effective (in form of 100% population tests), auditors must
develop end further educate themselves with, among others, Data Analytics.
“If I were at university now and got a second chance to study, then I would read computer science as
a minor subject so that you would become familiar with different tools.” Interviewee M.

An auditor does not need to have analytical skills to perform his or her job, but it
makes the auditor dependent on others, which means you might not entirely
understand what has been done (interviewee G). Technically, this means an
understanding of where the information is coming from, how to extract the data, and
what can be done with the data (interviewees G, E). Another manager explained that
trend analysis is something that the future auditor should be more knowledgeable
about. Further, an understanding of the whole process, starting from the database,
and then data cleansing and organising, is fairly simple but still definitely a skill that
an auditor needs. Lastly, an auditor should know how to visualise data, which will

61
probably in the future become the most important skill (interviewee B). A sidenote
can be mentioned here that the manager data specialist(B) was convinced that
knowledge in excel will not be enough in the future.
"Excel skills will certainly be as important the upcoming x years, but Excel skills alone will not be
enough in the future, that is a fact." (B)

All agreed that this need to be educated more, but there were disagreements on
whose responsibility it was. Four of the interviewed auditors thought that is the
educational institutions' responsibility to educate the future auditors in Data
Analytics and possibly BDA. The rest thought it was a mixture of universities giving
the base layer, but then the audit instructions responsibility to further educate, that
it's the responsibility of the hiree to ensure that the work-force is up to speed.
Senior associate E stressed that those who have the role of Data Specialist should
have gotten a separate education regarding analytics, and be hired to perform
analytics. Every auditor has a responsibility themselves to be educated, but it's the
audit companies and the educator's responsibility to highlight that this type of
knowledge is needed.
Position-practice
The interviewees seemed to agree that the expectation of the positioning data
specialist to be someone who has extensive knowledge in Data Analytics, but not
how this knowledge is acquired or whose responsibility it is to educate the data
specialist. The praxis of implementing capabilities seemed to vary between it being
a political power, meaning it was the educational system that would be responsible
for the expansion of capabilities, or economic, meaning it was the audit company’s
responsibility to invest in resources and technologies to insert Data Analytics into its
praxis through capabilities. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)

The future role of DA and BDA
All the interviewees expected DA not only to have a role in the future audit but a
larger role in the audit. A wish made by interviewee A was that all the manual work
that can be, should be automized and that the work of the auditor should be reduced
to only using professional knowledge and not excel and data management.
Interviewee C hoped that the whole population inspection would become normalised

62
and that the data department would get a larger role in handling and analysing the
data, while the auditor concentrated on the inspection itself. As for Big Data
Analytics, the opinion remained: all expected BDA to have a role in the future audit,
even if it comes one baby step at a time.
“The audit must be going there (referencing BDA), it is not just about what is now, but what is at
stake when it came to artificial intelligence and other robotics, and this textual analysis and the rest is
an important thing, I believe so.” Interviewee B

Position-practice
The interviewees had trust in that they could assume the change in the audit process
regarding Data Analytics and Big Data Analytics to be constant, indicating that they
trusted the praxis they were within to be able to handle and utilise the upcoming
changes. The third element of capabilities is also evident, where the interviewees
could envisage future relations with Data Analytics, and Big Data Analytics, within
the structure. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)
Findings
The research question What type of skillset is required of the next generation of
auditors in terms of analytical skills aimed to understand what precisely is required
of the auditor in the future regarding the current skillset. It was clear that the current
skillset is not adequate: all the interviewees concluded that current auditors are not
equipped for the analytics performed, and in combination with the expectation that
the role of Data Analytics in the audit process will grow. They stressed that the
miscommunication in what is possible with Data Analytics has led to unused
analysis and seemed sometimes to be more a statement to the clients than a
requirement for the audit.
They explained that the current academic education, referring to the education of
graduated from the last ten years, has not been adequate for the Data Analytics
performed. Up till this point, this has not been an issue, since this is a fairly new
technology in the audit process, but the need for academic institutions to, if not
educate, then at least highlight the need for an auditor that not only understand
auditing and accounting, but also analytics.
The skills required would be data cleaning and organising, data visualisation and
different types of trend analyses, as well as familiarity with the different analytics

63
programs such as Alteryx and Tableau. This is especially important to emphasise
since DA will not only remain within the audit but will have a bigger role in the
audit process. However, whether this applies to all auditors or a branch of auditors
specialised in Data Analytics, is not yet clear, and will certainly not become clear for
many years. Regardless, the auditor cannot begin to utilise the possibilities that BDA
bring before the auditors are comfortable with Data Analytics, especially since the
distinction between the two is not yet clear.
Position-practice
Through the position-practice viewpoint, the combination of disagreement in the
education of capabilities and the belief that that Data Analytics will only have a
larger role in the future, the trust in the capabilities and praxis seemed to be
somewhat misplaced when it came to the implementation of the positioning data
specialist and the upcoming Big Data Analytics. The interviewees trusted that the
development of analytics within the audit process to be constant, that the expansion
will continue, but they did not have a plan for what this meant for the capabilities of
the audit team. (Coad, Glyptis 2014)

64
6. ANALYSIS
In this chapter, an analysis performed of the result of the study concerning previous
research in this area. The analysis is sectioned into two main areas – the actual
implementation and role of (B)DA in the audit process, by comparing what previous
research has concluded in combination with the conducted study, as well the current
and future skillset of the auditor in terms of analytical skills and future
implementation of (B)DA in auditing.
6.1. The role of (B)DA in the audit process – study versus previous research
The audits at the Big Four were conducted in the same way as Riistama (2000)
suggested, in three stages called planning, execution and conclusion. It was
concluded by the interviewees that DA was mostly used at the planning stage,
sometimes the conclusion, but seldom at the execution stage, since it is often used as
an effort to increase familiarity with the company when the audit is designed.
Dagilienė and Klovienė (2019) stressed that (B)DA usually came as part of the
company strategy of IT innovations, with the main purpose of “ensuring the quality
of the audit process”. In this case, it is partly true: (B)DA was introduced as a part of
the strategy for IT innovation, but there the similarities end. While the (B)DA
increases audit quality, none of the interviewees thought it necessary to ensure audit
quality. BDA is merely a tool for increasing the possibility of a consistent highquality audit, not a necessity for high-quality audits. Klovienė and Dagiliene (2019)
also concluded that BDA techniques were used because audit clients created and
allowed for suitable conditions to use Big Data, in the form of financial and nonfinancial, structured and non-structured data, for audit purposes. The interviewees
observed how, in recent years, there has been a significant change in the format of
materials which the clients deliver, and that auditors now understand to demand,
which has been the main reason why it is even possible to use Data Analytics in the
audit. The type of data, however, is limited to structural data.
Krahel and Titera (2015) commented further on this subject that while companies are
increasingly automating their processes, deeper data analysis in combination with
data availability must be introduced into the auditing process to ensure a more
thorough and higher-quality audit. This is seemingly unnecessary since the

65
interviewees dismissed the notion that the current audit is lacking to the point that
methods such as Data Analytics are needed, but almost all the interviewees
considered themselves able to attain a comprehensive picture of the client and did
not consider the amount of information to be an issue which needs more analytical
techniques to solve.
In 2019, Tang and Karim stressed that BDA could yield more insightful and reliable
results which, in turn, improves the decision-making process. For example, auditors
could conduct regression analysis on the whole population, instead of merely the
sampling size and, therefore, improve the model’s usage and explanatory power, as
well as the identification of anomalies. This was a wish expressed by most
interviewees to introduce whole population analysis into the audit process, and some
concluded that they already conduct whole population analysis in some capacity.
Cao, Chychyla and Stewart (2015) further confirmed that the best application for
BDA would be the ability to test the entire population instead of basing the audit
opinion on sampling. This is in line with the conducted study, as the interviewees
considered the main motivation of company X to introduce BDA to be able to
produce a more extensive result, based on the majority of the data that must be
inspected, rather than presenting to the client results based on only a portion of the
data. An issue regarding this was, though, that when conducting whole population
analysis and investigating anomalies, the number of anomalies could very well be
astronomical depending on the criteria for anomalies, rendering the whole population
analysis inefficient and problematic.
Rose and Rose (2015) explained that large audit companies such as Big Four
consider BD as an ever-increasing “essential part of their assurance practice”, which
was not in line with the study conducted. I had to explain what BD was to most of
the interviewees, even Data Specialists, and most considered it to be something in
the future, however, not now. This is further proven by the fact that the interviewees
did not consider even DA to be essential to the audit just yet (partly due to the
underutilisation of DA yet), but merely a tool. If an audit were to be conducted
without it, the auditor report would be of equal standard and quality as one
conducted without DA. According to (Klovienė, Dagiliene, 2018) “business
companies had used BDA tools for more than five years” and considered this as an
“increasing trend in the future because of strong competition, and these tools were

66
used to understand the customers’ behaviour, to manage risk and for internal
management purposes”. This contradicts the results of the study, while at the same
time enforces the statement that BDA and DA might be the same thing, due to the
fact that DA has been used at the company X for 3-4 years, and is only now
beginning to take a concrete shape. However, the reason for increasing BDA, or in
this case introducing, would be reducing audit risk, reduce the workload of the
auditor and provide a better service for the auditor.
Specific areas for application of BDA would according to Tang and Karim (2019)
and Wanner and Janiech (2019) in areas such fraud detection and sustainability
reporting. The study supports this in part: an interviewee explained with the help of
the upcoming XBRL reporting requirement, BDA would be applicable in using
external data in form of XBRL when investigating fraud and other grey economy
practices would aid and improve the probability of detection.
Alles and Gray (2016) defined Big Data as having data sources from external
sources using techniques such as visualisation and predictive analysis. Most of the
interviewees concluded that they used Data Analytics techniques with traditional
data sources, such as visualisation and text mining, but note extended sources such
as non-financial data on a larger scale. In this context, the previously mentioned
XBRL would be an appropriate example of an external non-financial data source to
use in the audit, as concluded by interviewee D.

6.2. The current and future auditors competence and requirement in the audit
Dagilienė and Klovienė (2019) explained that (B)DA usually came as part of the
company strategy of IT innovations, with one purpose described as being able “...to
issue a relevant auditor’s report”. This does not comply with the result of the study,
as none of the interviewees considered additional methods to be necessary to obtain
a better overview of the audited company. However, a problem mentioned by some
of the interviewees was that even though the amount of information to review is vast,
the issue was not the amount of data, but the different formats of data and how it is
organised by the client. This is an area where expert systems and predictive analysis
could be of great help, according to Brown-Liburd, Issa, and Lombardi (2015).

67
Krahel and Titera (2015) stressed that the auditing standards continue to focus on
methods such as sampling and that these practices are no longer relevant in a dataintensive environment and, therefore, standards must address “atomistic errors and
their appropriate response”. This is partly in line with the conducted study, with
some of the interviewees complaining about the inefficiency of the sample-based
audit, with one manager pointing out that the sample-based audit is not suitable for
Data Analytics. Rose et al. (2015) agree with the latter statement by pointing out
how Big Data has the potential to completely change the way auditors collect
evidence and make audit decisions: since the interviewees do not believe that DA
will decrease in importance within the audit, but instead predict that its usage will
increase, the way the audit is conducted might need a change to make room for DA.
As a method to increase DA in the audit process, the interviewees considered
visualisation to be an area which could be utilised far more than it is now, conducive
with Rose and Rose’s (2015) study which found that “data visualisation belongs to
one of the fastest-growing practice areas at the larger offices of the Big Four firms”.
Another statement by the interviewees was the need for automation of manual
actions so that the auditor can concentrate on the audit rather than having multiple
discussions with the client to understand the client. This supports the statement by
Brown-Libburd, Issa and Lombardi (2015), who considered that the time an auditor
allocates to familiarise himself with a company in response to auditing a changing
business phenomenon manually is wasteful in terms of time and largely unnecessary.
Were data-collection procedures to transition from manual processes, such as
reviewing company records and financial statements, to automatic processes, such as
natural language processing, the auditor would have more time to concentrate on
more complex tasks. The interviewees agreed that text analysis of the different
contracts (sale and purchase contracts and board minutes) would most definitely
decrease the time the auditor uses to go through contracts and, instead, allow for the
allocation of time towards the analysis of the contracts.
An issue that Griffin and Wright (2015) mentioned was the lack of adequately
trained specialists in auditing Big Data-based ERP systems, a statement the study
can support: even though the Data specialists are the ones meant to be the ones who
would drive (big) Data Analytics forward, some stressed that the training the

68
company X provided was insufficient, and that to take Data Analytics to the next
level, they thought that the level of expertise had to be far higher than it is now.
The level of DA the conducted study found in auditing is clearly in line with the
statement Grepp, Linnenlucke, O’Niell and Smith (2018) said in 2018, that the
auditing profession is lagging in the use of Big Data techniques, the profession has a
large and growing, the volume of data of significant variety and veracity to review. It
is not false that the amount of data is significant, but the auditors do not, at least yet,
consider this to be an issue. I must explain, however, that because there is a fairly
large gap in knowledge about the possible utilisation of Data Analytics in auditing
among the ones deciding to use DA, there may be a fair amount of prejudice against
not only new technology but the ability to objectively criticise the current methods in
the audit process.
An issue mentioned by Tang, and Karim (2019) and Shukla and Mattar (2019) was
that issues with introducing Big Data Analytics was operating costs, such as keeping
the systems up to date, the analysis of the data, the collection of the various types of
data and the storage of the collected data. This did not come up among the
interviewees but instead thought the problem with implementing BDA to be lack of
knowledge among the auditors and lack of data.
All the interviewees thought the current auditors to be underqualified when it comes
to Data Analytics. This supports the issue Brown-Libburd and Issa explained that
one of the major challenges in implementing BDA is just that there is a “lack of the
adequate training and required skills to analyse Big Data”. If Data Analytics already
has issues in regard to competence, Big Data most certainly does not have the
expertise required. The auditors that were end-users seemed to order the analytics,
without a clear plan for the usage of the results, and some of the data specialists felt
that the work they did was left unused and that the end-users had no idea what the
data specialists did. This is a major issue regarding the further implementation of
(B)DA, as Brown-Libburd, Issa and Lombardi explained, that new tools into an
already time-constrained process cause problems incorrect utilisation. In this case,
the same type of advantage that Big Data provides the auditor pose an equally great
challenge: being able to use Big Data and Big Data Analytics for audit judgement
and decision-making when the end-user does not understand to point of it.

69
Alles and Gray (2016) stressed that there must be a change in the
analytical/technical skills of the audit team, and in a study conducted by Klovienė
and Dagiliene, on motivation to use BDA, an interviewed auditor disclosed that it is
not enough for the auditor to have and accounting an audit education, but they must
also have an IT competence. This continues to be true, according to true the
conducted study: all the interviewees agreed that it is not enough anymore to merely
have skills in auditing and accounting, but it is beginning to become clear that the
auditor needs to have at least a basis in analytics. Shuckla and Mattar (2019)
continue by stating that it also seems to be a trend for the audit companies to
underinvest in information technology, something that this study does not support,
given the fact that the company has invested in several new programs in the year of
2020, and has also allocated to every employee five days a year to improve their data
and digital skills.
A type of analytical skill that was suggested by the interviewees was the capability to
manage, clean and visualise data, as well as understand how to conduct regression
analysis and when to use it. Rose et al (2015) also explained how current auditors do
not understand and adequately use historical information to identify patterns and use
their findings for their audit judgement. This is something that will with high
probability be a requirement for the auditor to understand to be able to fully perform
their duties as an auditor.

70
7. CONCLUDING REMARKS
This chapter is the final chapter of this thesis. It gives the reader suggestions for
future studies in the first chapter, limitations with the thesis in the second chapter
and lastly the final discussion, which reflects on the audit industry in its current
climate.
7.1. Future studies
This thesis has given light to many types of potential future studies. Some auditors
commented that the current sample-based audit does not allow for analytics, and,
therefore, it would be interesting to research whether the sample-based audit is
adequate or should whole population audits be introduced.
Another investigation would be towards the current education of the auditor: does
the audit education adequately prepare the auditor for the working life, or should it
prepare the auditor for the working life. It would also be interesting to investigate the
responsibility of the team leader in the audit engagement, if the quality of the audit is
affected by the type of auditor that leads the audit, in relation to going concerns
given or reposts on flailing audit quality.
Since Data Analytics in auditing still seems to be in a transition phase, this study
should be repeated in about five years, as many auditors commented that they expect
all auditors to be knowledgeable in this subject in five to ten years. As for future
studies within auditing and Data Analytics, one would be on the implementation of
XBRL affects company comparability, and if it would aid in going concern decision
making. Another would be the necessity to use Data Analytics when getting a new
client, with all the issues in no previous history with the client arise.
7.2. Limitations
This thesis has many limitations. Since it is a case study, it is limited to only one
company, and cannot be used as a benchmark. Since it is qualitative, is based on the
interviewee's perspectives, and, therefore, lose objectivity comparability. I am not an
expert in Data Analytics, Big Data Analytics nor am I a statistician, and, therefore,
my perspective might not be academic enough.

71
7.3. Final discussion
The result suggests that there is a gap of knowledge between the audit team member
that orders the data analysis, and the team member that performs the data analysis. If
audit companies are to implement Data Analytics, it cannot only be the entry-level
that is educated but the very top as well. If Big Data Analytics is coming, the whole
audit team, including the manager of the team, must be equally aware of both the
opportunities and pitfalls, of Big Data Analytics.
The audit industry in Finland is currently in a transition phase that has yet to settle,
resulting in this study being made maybe a few years too early. If I would have
conducted this study a year ago, I would most likely have gotten quite a different
kind of result. The purpose of this study was to evaluate what type of role Big Data
Analytics and Data Analytics has within the audit process, and what type of impact it
has on the auditor and the future of auditors' skill in the audit process. The
motivation of this thesis is to establish how analytics is utilised and how it impacts
the auditor, and how that affects what the future of auditing looks like concerning
Data Analytics. I believe this thesis managed to achieve the purpose and motivation
of the study.
The audit industry is equally static and moving – the same investigation is
performed, on the same industries, but with the businesses ever-changing in
organisational methods and effectiveness, so must the auditor adapt. We cannot
stand still and watch as the world develops around us, but the question of how to
assimilate to change is a difficult one. Should the responsibility lay on the auditor to
educate themselves, should the responsibility be on the educational facilitates to
produce knowledgeable auditors or is it the audit companies' incentive to have a
professional audit staff to dictate the level they want to perform at?
There are no straight answers, only trials of those who execute in a desire to do
better. How the audit process is structured might be the audit companies'
responsibility, how the inspection is executed would be reliant on the auditors
themselves, and the knowledge to base this upon could come from the educational
institutions. The current gap in knowledge between executors and end-users indicate
that this responsibility is not clear, and when responsibility is not delegated,
problems, which in this case are gaps in knowledge, arise.
